Received 
      1st September 2018
    First published on 11th February 2019AbstractOver the past decade, electrochemical energy storage (EES) devices have greatly improved, as a wide variety of advanced electrode active materials and new device architectures have been developed. These new materials and devices should be evaluated against clear and rigorous metrics, primarily based on the evidence of real performances. A series of criteria are commonly used to characterize and report performance of EES systems in the literature. However, as advanced EES systems are becoming more and more sophisticated, the methodologies to reliably evaluate the performance of the electrode active materials and EES devices need to be refined to realize the true promise as well as the limitations of these fast-moving technologies, and target areas for further development. In the absence of a commonly accepted core group of metrics, inconsistencies may arise between the values attributed to the materials or devices and their real performances. Herein, we provide an overview of the energy storage devices from conventional capacitors to supercapacitors to hybrid systems and ultimately to batteries. The metrics for evaluation of energy storage systems are described, although the focus is kept on capacitive and hybrid energy storage systems. In addition, we discuss the challenges that still need to be addressed for establishing more sophisticated criteria for evaluating EES systems. We hope this effort will foster ongoing dialog and promote greater understanding of these metrics to develop an international protocol for accurate assessment of EES systems.

Abolhassan Noori received his PhD under the supervision of Professor A. Abbaspour from Shiraz University in 2011. During his PhD he spent 6 months in the group of (late) Prof. Marco Mascini at the University of Florence, Italy, as a Visiting Student Researcher, working on the implementation of nanostructured materials in biosensors. Mousavi. In 2015, he began pursuing Electrochemical Energy Storage (EES) systems. Recently, Dr Noori joined the Department of Chemistry at TMU as a Research Associate Professor, working with Prof. Mousavi to continue expanding his expertise and knowledge in energy storage/conversion systems. He is a fellow of the Iranian Chemical Society (ICS) and Iranian Nanotechnology Society (INS). His current research interests focus on the rational design of nanostructured materials, bioinspired from nature, to solve problems in bioanalytical chemistry and materials science, ranging from challenges in targeted drug delivery to energy-related applications.

His groundbreaking work on graphene supercapacitors led to the development of Nanotech Energy, Inc. where he is leading a team of scientists and engineers for developing more powerful batteries, supercapacitors, printed electronics, nanomaterials, conductive inks and adhesives. Introduction
Due to limited crude oil resources and growing concerns about their negative environmental impacts, particularly global warming and the emission of toxic chemicals, there is an increasing interest in renewable energy. Switching from fossil-based energy to renewable energy offers the possibility of saving energy and simplifying energy distribution by sending electrons over the grid, or even generating the energy from renewable sources on-site, instead of transporting fossil-based fuels. However, intermittency of renewable energy sources such as sunlight, wind, and tide calls for energy storage to ensure efficient, continuous, reliable and affordable energy supplies. Electrochemical energy storage (EES) technologies along with materials development constitute essential elements to tackle the challenges to provide a stable supply of energy. Over the past decade, steady progress has been made towards this goal through the development of advanced electrode active materials. Thus, high power supercapacitors and high-energy rechargeable batteries have been developed. However, each of these stand-alone technologies by itself lacks the requisite qualities to meet the commercial needs that have been shaped by the consumption of fossil fuels. Consequently, hybrid energy storage devices that strive to advance along the diagonal of a so-called Ragone plot, by combining the high power characteristics of supercapacitors with the high energy content of rechargeable batteries, have been explored1 (Fig. 1).
 Fig. 1  Ragone plot showing the specific power vs. specific energy of various energy storage devices. Adapted with permission from ref. 5, copyright 2017, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH and Co. with modifications. 
The introduction of advanced nanostructures for a new generation of energy storage systems has contributed to the blurring of the distinctions between batteries and supercapacitors based on two fundamentally different energy-storage mechanisms. However, increasing the energy content of supercapacitors to approach that of batteries, or improving the power performance of batteries to approach that of supercapacitors, usually comes at the cost of losses in power and/or cyclability of supercapacitors and deteriorations in the energy of batteries.2,3 Thus, the outstanding properties reported for some devices, e.g. supercapacitors with energy densities that approach or even exceed that of batteries,4 do not necessarily translate into reality. Although some metrics seem to support the claims of having both high power and high energy densities, the actual performance of a given device almost always contradicts the assertions being made. Thus, in spite of many attempts that have been made to standardize the evaluation methods of EES systems, there is still a lack of clear rules for evaluating the performance of EES systems in a fair and systematic way.
Herein, we first present an introduction to the basic principles of different energy storage systems. Then we describe some of the most important and commonly employed characterization techniques to guide newcomers of the EES community as well as scientists and engineers who may not be familiar with the electrochemical terms through the steps that are necessary to effectively and accurately evaluate their EES systems. We hope this effort will serve as a resourceful starting point, and as a step forward along with the efforts that others have made, to sharpen the distinction between different charge storage modalities. We also expect that the push into the evaluation of EES systems with appropriate metrics that give a real picture of the performance of EES systems will help in reaching a point where we can all speak the same language.
Overview of energy storage technologies
Electrical energy storage technology can be classified by the form of storage into a variety of categories including mechanical energy storage (e.g. flywheels), chemical energy storage and conversion (e.g. water splitting, fuel cells), thermal and thermochemical energy storage (e.g. heat storage, solar hydrogen, solar thermal energy), as well as electrical energy storage (e.g. physical capacitors, electrical double layer capacitors) and electrochemical energy storage (e.g. pseudocapacitors, batteries).6 Herein, we keep the focus on capacitors, supercapacitors, and batteries that are three different classes of energy storage devices, which share some similarities and have several key differences. These are different technologies with different capabilities and of course different applications. These energy storage devices are generally complementary (rather than competing) and combining them, especially a supercapacitor and a battery, should be considered an opportunity to help guarantee that the needs of our energy future can be met.7,8
Physical capacitors represent the first and simplest form of an energy storage device. They are usually made up of two parallel plates separated by a thin insulating material known as the dielectric (Fig. 2a). One plate of the capacitor has a positive charge, while the other maintains a negative charge. Thus, in a capacitor, the charge becomes stored in the form of an electric potential energy between the two plates. Electrolytic capacitors are considered as the latest evolutionary step of capacitor technology. In electrolytic capacitors the dielectric is replaced with an ionic (but not electronic) conductive medium, which is also often called the electrolyte.9 In an electrolytic capacitor the ions are freely mobile in the bulk of the electrolyte, thus providing a much greater charge density at the electrode/electrolyte interface than is possible at the electrode/dielectric interface.9 Therefore, the capacitance of electrolytic capacitors is often in the mF range, but only in the μF range for dielectric capacitors.
 Fig. 2  Schematic representation of a conventional capacitor (a) and a supercapacitor (b). The detailed structure of the electrical double layer is presented as insets. 
Supercapacitors (also known as electrochemical capacitors or ultracapacitors) do not use conventional dielectrics between the plates; instead, they consist of two electrodes separated by an ion-permeable separator (such as cardboard, ceramic, glass, plastic or paper) and an electrolyte that ionically connects the two electrodes (Fig. 2b). In a supercapacitor, the charge becomes stored either by separation of charge in a Helmholtz double layer at the electrode–electrolyte interface (known as the electrical or electrochemical double layer capacitance, EDLC) or by Faradaic charge-transfer via redox reactions (known as pseudocapacitance).10,11
Batteries are another intriguing class of energy storage devices. Batteries consist of two electrodes separated by an electrolyte. However, batteries do not actually store electrical energy. Batteries are devices that store energy in the form of chemical energy inside electrode active materials via chemical bonds, and convert the chemical energy into direct current electricity via redox reactions or via intercalation as with Li-ion batteries.12 In a battery, the energy is stored in a particular compound or compounds that make up the negative and positive poles, as well as the electrolyte.
Along with the two most popular research areas focusing on batteries and supercapacitors, in recent years, some supercapacitor-type electrode materials have been coupled with battery-type materials to produce hybrid energy storage systems, for which the term supercapattery (= supercapacitor + battery) has been coined.1,13,14 In hybrid energy storage devices, two electrodes with different charge storage behaviors, e.g. one capacitive (supercapacitor) and one Faradaic (battery), are combined, where the former serves as the power source, while the latter serves as the energy source. The behavior of a hybrid system resembles that of a rechargeable battery with a higher power and/or cycle life, or a supercapacitor with a higher energy.1 Such an integration offers opportunities for further advancement in the energy storage realm, and performance of such devices is generally in between that of a supercapacitor and a battery.
In general, electrical energy storage devices, like a hydroelectric power plant (Fig. 3), give potential energy to the charges.
 Fig. 3  A hydroelectric power plant is analogous to an electrical energy storage system. Water from the upper reservoir flows through the pipes to turn a turbine generator, the same way that electrons flow through the wires of a battery from the negative pole to the positive one to produce light. The inset shows the energy diagram of the devices. The symbols c and a are the electrochemical potentials of the electrons (i.e. the Fermi energy of the electrons) of the negative pole (the anode during the discharge step) and the positive pole (the cathode during the discharge step), respectively. Vg is the voltage window of the electrolyte. VOC is the open-circuit voltage of the device. 
A pumped storage hydropower technology gives gravitational potential energy to water, while electrical energy storage devices give electrical potential energy to their charges. In a pumped storage plant, water is pumped from a lower reservoir to an upper reservoir. Then, water flows through the pipes from the upper reservoir to the lower one. While passing through the pipes, the gravitational potential energy of water is changed into kinetic energy by turning a rotor, and then the kinetic energy is partially converted into electrical energy using a generator. In an energy storage device, charges flow through wires and their potential energy is converted to e.g., light energy or thermal energy, by a bulb or a resistor, respectively. Analogous to a water pump, the charges that have lost all their potential energy receive a potential boost from a power provider (like the pump) and the cycle begins again.
Generally, electrical charge can be stored either on the surface (double layer charging) or in the bulk of a material (Faradaic reactions). Thus, an ideal material for an EDLC must have a highly accessible effective surface area. For example, the charge storage in carbon-based materials is founded on the accumulation of electrical charge at the interface between the electrode active material and the electrolyte, giving rise to an EDLC (Fig. 4a). In this process, no Faradaic reactions take place across the electrode/electrolyte interface and no electrochemical reactions take place inside the bulk of the electrode active material. In rechargeable batteries, charge storage occurs through reversible Faradaic reactions throughout the bulk of the electrode materials in combination with the electrolyte (Fig. 4b). Thus, the material must be designed to allow the easy insertion/de-insertion of the reacting species throughout the material. Charge storage in pseudocapacitors is based on Faradaic redox reactions on the surface or near the surface of the electrode active materials, without ion diffusion within the bulk of the electrode active materials (Fig. 4c). In this case, l, the diffusion length, is much smaller than the thickness of the diffusion layer, i.e., where l is the diffusion length, D is the diffusion coefficient and t is time. As the name implies, hybrid energy storage systems are a hybrid between batteries and supercapacitors, with a behavior intermediate between EDLC and Faradaic processes as the two extremes (Fig. 4d).
 Fig. 4  Different electrochemical energy storage systems. Schematic representation of (a) an electric double layer capacitor (EDLC), (b) a battery, (c) a pseudocapacitor, and (d) a hybrid system that comprises an EDLC and an intercalative charge storage process. 
It should be pointed out that both EDLC and pseudocapacitive charge storage processes are capacitive in nature and/or behavior, with near-rectangular cyclic voltammograms (CVs) or quasi-triangular galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) profiles, and the prefix “pseudo-” just reflects the difference in the mechanism of charge storage. On the other hand, well-defined peak-shaped CVs or nonlinear GCD profiles with one or more obvious potential plateaus are characteristics of rechargeable batteries and/or hybrid devices. Thus, different charge storage mechanisms can be categorized as (1) capacitive non-Faradaic charge storage (CNFS), (2) capacitive Faradaic charge storage (CFS), and (3) non-capacitive Faradaic charge storage (NCFS). The correct metrics for characterization of capacitive charge storage processes is capacitance (F, i.e. q/V) and that of non-capacitive Faradaic processes is the specific charge capacity (mA h, i.e. q/3.6). Thus, if the correct evaluation criteria had been used, many of the extremely high specific capacitance values derived from peak shaped CVs that have been reported in the literature would not have been exciting at all.
Now, the question is how to differentiate among the three charge storage mechanisms. It is clear what EDLCs are and how they differ from Faradaic charge storage processes. The confusion arises, however, when researchers try to distinguish between batteries and pseudocapacitors, both of which are Faradaic processes in nature. Two key features that help differentiate between the two mechanisms are15–17 (1) phase transformation(s) of the host material(s) in batteries that translates into peak-shaped CVs and plateaus in GCD profiles, where the pseudocapacitive material(s) either present rectangular shaped CVs or CVs with significantly broadened peaks; (2) intrinsic kinetics of the Faradaic processes where the redox reactions in batteries are diffusion-controlled processes (i.e. I ∝ ν0.5), while in pseudocapacitors the current is directly proportional to the scan rate (I ∝ ν). Hybrid energy storage systems show a behavior in between a capacitor and a battery. Contributions from each of the above-mentioned categories can be identified by analyzing the CV data, which is explained in detail subsequently. Contributions from EDLC and pseudocapacitance to the total capacitive charge storage can also be deconvoluted by step potential electrochemical spectroscopy techniques.

Table 1 illustrates and summarizes the main characteristics of energy storage systems including physical capacitors, supercapacitors (either EDLCs or pseudocapacitors), hybrid energy storage systems and batteries that distinguish them from each other.

Basic principles of physical capacitors

Constant current charging–discharging a capacitor. 
To gain a deeper understanding of energy storage systems, and of how to design and develop more efficient ones, fundamental principles of the capacitive energy storage phenomenon need to be intensively investigated. We can utilize system theory for analysis of the energy storage process, since this provides an analytical framework for viewing an energy storage device as a unified whole (Fig. 5).
 Fig. 5  System theory as a way of studying the energy storage process in its entirety. In such a system, the input could be the voltage or current that is applied to the energy storage device and the output could be the current or voltage that can be readily monitored. The feedback of the output to the input could provide information about the system under study, e.g. the minimum and maximum cut-off voltages. 
A capacitor is an energy storage device. It accumulates energy by storing electric charge on two sides of a break in a conductor. The most basic model is that of a parallel plate capacitor. As electric charges (electrons) move from one plate to another, a positive charge, +q, builds up on one plate and a negative charge, −q, builds up on the other. In the initial stage of charging, electrons move freely from the positive plate to the negative plate. As time passes, the electrons that accumulate on the negative plate repel the incoming electrons. These later electrons encounter even more repulsion. Thus, as the time continues, the potential across the plates rises. Now, consider what happens when the direction of the applied current is reversed. The electrons stored on the capacitor flow through the external circuit and this process continues until the capacitor is fully discharged and the voltage across the plates of the capacitor drops to zero.
The fundamental equation that describes the operation of a capacitor is: q(t) = CV(t)(1)in which q is the stored charge in Coulombs, C is the capacitance in Farads, and V is the voltage between the terminals of the capacitor in volts. The subscript t indicates the time dependency of the parameters. By differentiating each side of the equation with time, we obtain: (2)Note that capacitance is assumed to be constant, thus, it is not part of the differentiation. On the other hand: (3)From the definition of constant current charging/discharging; i(t) = i. Thus, (4)In practice, the capacitance of a capacitor depends on the voltage. However, assuming a linear capacitor in which the capacitance is constant: (5) (6)Assuming that t1 = 0 and Vi = 0; (7)This means that the voltage across the plate of the capacitor will change linearly with time and the rate or slope of the change is i/C (Fig. 6a). It also shows that:
 Fig. 6  
V–t (a) and V–q (b) plots of the output signal of an ideal capacitor charged with a constant current, i, for a definite time, t, and then discharged with the same current. 
The higher the capacitance, the more sluggish the voltage across the capacitor changes.
The higher the current, the quicker the voltage across the capacitor changes.
The charging and discharging processes depend on the sign or direction of the current. Obviously, if the current flows into the capacitor (charging), then the voltage will increase, if current flows out of the capacitor (discharge), then the voltage will decrease.
From what is presented in Fig. 6a, the time of charging, tc, and discharging, td, that can be obtained from the x-axis in the figure, gives us some information about the characteristics of the capacitor. The period of time that the capacitor can do work can be obtained from the plot. Moreover, equality of tc and td indicates that the coulombic efficiency of the device is 100% (i.e. qc = qd), with almost unlimited cyclability. The operating potential window of the capacitor can be deduced from the y-axis of the plot. The potential across the plates of the capacitor increases from Vi to a maximum value of Vf. However, to get more useful information out of the plot, the variables of both axes should be simultaneously taken into consideration. For example, the total energy that a capacitor stores during the charging process and delivers when discharging is one of the most important parameters. The stored and delivered energy of a capacitor can be obtained by integrating the area under the V–q plot (i.e. the area of the triangle), during the charge and discharge processes (Fig. 6b). The slope of the plot is the reciprocal of capacitance, 1/C, of the capacitor. One of the main advantages of capacitors as energy storage devices is that they can quickly store and deliver a large amount of electricity. The advantage of being able to be charged and discharged almost instantaneously means that capacitors are high power energy storage devices since power is the rate of energy transfer, i.e. the amount of energy that is stored or delivered per unit of time, as given in eqn (8). (8)


Energy conservation in charging a capacitor. 
If a total charge of q flows through an energy storage device, then the total work (W) done by the device is: W = qV = CV2(9)The stored energy (E) in the capacitor is the work carried out to charge up the capacitor with a charge q: (10)The other half of the energy is dissipated by the resistance R of the circuit: (11) (12)

Electrochemical energy storage devices
Historical background of electrochemical energy storage
Batteries are by far the most common form of energy storage devices. The ancient Persians had probably known about electricity before it was officially discovered.57 The “Parthian Battery”, which was found in 1936 during archaeological excavations near Baghdad, appeared to be a prehistoric battery that dates back to the Parthian empire (247 B.C.–224 A.D.).58 The battery consisted of a clay jar filled with a vinegar solution into which an iron rod surrounded by a cylinder made of a rolled copper sheet was inserted. The jar might have been used to generate electricity (1.1 to 2.0 V) for the purpose of silver and gold plating. The history of modern batteries can be traced back to around 1800 when Alessandro Volta developed the so-called voltaic pile, consisting of alternating discs of zinc and copper separated by cardboard.59 The Volta cell evolved into the two-electrolyte Daniel cell in 1836, and then into the Leclanche cell in 1866 which used a zinc anode and a carbon cathode.60 The next major milestone was the development of rechargeable (secondary) batteries which began with the invention of the lead–acid battery in 1859 and then evolved over the years through nickel–cadmium (1899) and nickel–metal hydride (patented in 1986) to Li-ion batteries59 (announced in 1972 by Whittingham; turned into a breakthrough in energy storage by Goodenough in the 1980s; and commercialized in the 1990s).
The beginning of capacitor technology is generally attributed to the invention of the Leyden jar in 1745.7 Next, paper capacitors, made by sandwiching a strip of wax-impregnated or metalized paper between two metal foils and rolled up tightly into a cylinder, were invented in 1876.61 Aluminum and tantalum are the so-called “valve metals” that develop a stable passivating oxide layer (Al2O3 and Ta2O5) when immersed in an appropriate electrolyte.62 Valve metals are able to conduct electricity in one direction, but impede the current flow in the opposite direction as a result of the formation of oxide dielectrics. On account of this property, the first electrolytic capacitor, based on a high-purity etched aluminum foil with aluminum oxide as a dielectric, was patented in 1896.62 Other notable capacitors include the mica dielectric capacitor invented in 1909, the polyethylene terephthalate based capacitor patented in 1941, and plastic dielectric capacitors developed beginning in 1959. The first patent describing the concept of electric double-layer charge storage was filed by General Electric in 1957 using porous carbon coated on a metallic current collector in a sulfuric acid solution. However, the device was highly impractical and never commercialized.61 The first commercial double layer capacitor (1.0 F, 5.5 V) was introduced in 1971 by Nippon Electric Corporation based on a patent filed by Standard Oil of Ohio.3 This can be considered as the starting point for the development and use of electrochemical capacitors in commercial devices. It was NEC, Inc. (Tokyo, Japan) that coined the term “supercapacitor” for the first commercially successful EDLC, and Pinnacle Research Institute (USA) that termed the device “ultracapacitor”,63 although currently electrochemical capacitors are generally referred to as supercapacitors or ultracapacitors (Note: within this review, the technology will be generally referred to by the term “electrochemical capacitors”, but we also use the term “supercapacitor” for the purpose of being consistent with the majority of the current literature). The concept of pseudocapacitance was first reported by Trasatti and Buzzanca in 1971,64 which opened up a new world of opportunities to enhance the specific capacitance of different systems. The first planar interdigitated micro-supercapacitor was reported in 2003 with polypyrrole electrodes.65 After the first micro-supercapacitor report, more and more innovative strategies,66 such as lithographic microfabrication,67 inkjet printing,68 layer-by-layer assembly,69 laser scribing,70,71 and screen printing,72 were developed. Despite many revolutionary developments from the Volta pile to lithium-ion batteries as well as advanced capacitive energy storage devices, each of the individual technologies still lags behind the demands of industry. A combination of the high energy of batteries and the high power and quick charging of supercapacitors offers opportunities for improved properties with respect to the individual components. Thus, many battery–supercapacitor hybrid devices have been developed during the past two decades.73–76 Later on, high-tech energy storage devices with more advanced functions and properties, such as being flexible, bendable, stretchable, foldable and wearable, were fabricated.77 This continuous research and development on battery-type and supercapacitive materials has led to today's high-performance energy storage devices. However, before getting down to the details of feature identification, a deeper understanding of the physico-chemical nature of the charge storage processes and the type of the electrode active material will help us steer a straight course and select the proper criteria for assessment.
Classification of electrochemical energy storage systems
Electrochemical energy storage systems are currently classified into three main categories: (1) batteries (including primary and secondary batteries), (2) supercapacitors, and (3) hybrid systems. Each energy storage cell/device contains a positive electrode, a negative electrode, an electrolyte, and a separator as well as current collectors and a casing (Fig. 7). Electrodes are active components of the energy storage system and are used for loading the active materials. They allow electrons to pass from one half-cell through an external circuit to the other. The electrolyte is a chemically stable liquid, gel or solid substance that provides conduction for the ions in the cell. In modern supercapacitors, the inert electrolytes can be replaced with redox electrolytes; either redox-active electrolytes which are inherently able to undergo Faradaic electron transfer reactions or redox-additive electrolytes in which redox active species are included to enable Faradaic electron transfer reactions at the electrode/electrolyte interfaces.78,79 A separator is a permeable membrane placed between the positive and negative terminals of the cell. The separator prevents instant self-discharge and electrical short circuits between the two electrodes, while allowing for the transport of ionic conductors that are essential for closing the circuit during the flow of current in the cell. However, by the use of either solid-state, polymer or gel electrolytes, the separator is no longer needed, which is considered to be an additional improvement in energy storage technology. Current collectors are used for the efficient transfer of electrons from the electrodes to the terminals of the battery. However, the development of highly conductive and freestanding electrode active materials, such as well-interconnected carbon nanotubes, graphene or 2D metal carbides with metallic conductivity, can eliminate the need for current collectors.15 The casing provides mechanical stability, protects the electrolyte from evaporation, and prevents negative influences from outside of the system.
 Fig. 7  Components of a typical energy storage device. Reproduced with permission from ref. 15, copyright 2016, Springer Nature. 
In many papers in the field of energy storage, the negative electrode is called the anode, while the positive electrode is called the cathode. Such a notation is probably an adaptation from primary batteries that can only be discharged, for which the anode is always the negative electrode and cathode is the positive one. A secondary cell, for example, a rechargeable battery, is a cell in which the chemical reactions are reversible. When a secondary cell, such as a rechargeable battery or a supercapacitor, is being charged, the anode becomes the positive terminal and the cathode the negative terminal. Thus, by calling an electrode the “anode”, it is impossible to know if it is the positive terminal or the negative terminal of the system. Note that the positive terminal always remains the positive end, and the negative terminal always remains the negative end. It is the anode/cathode designations that switch depending on whether the cell/device is charging or discharging. During the charging process, the negative pole of the cell/device is the cathode. During the discharge process, the situation reverses and the negative pole of the cell/device is the anode. To avoid misleading the readers, we use positive and negative notation throughout this article.
Batteries
Batteries are one of the energy storage devices that convert the chemical energy stored in their active electrode materials directly into electrical energy through electrochemical redox reactions or charge-transfer reactions. The reaction at the negative electrode gives up electrons to the external circuit and the reaction at the positive electrode accepts them. In a cell, the electrolyte provides a medium for the transfer of charge, as ions, between the positive and negative electrodes.

Classification of batteries. 
There are two fundamental types of batteries: (i) primary cells, commonly referred to as non-rechargeable batteries, and (ii) secondary cells, also known as rechargeable batteries.80 Within this classification, other sub-genres are also used to better classify batteries based on particular structures or designs.81
(a) Stationary vs. flow batteries
Conventional batteries employ non-flowing stationary electrolytes, whereas flow batteries contain two electrolyte solutions in two separate reservoirs that flow through or along the stationary electrodes. It should be noted that in stationary batteries, the charge is stored on solid electrode active materials, whereas in flow batteries the charge is stored on redox moieties dissolved in solution phases. The most common flow batteries are Zn–Br2 and V2+/V3+–V4+/V5+ batteries. One of the most advantageous properties of flow batteries is that they can be almost instantly recharged by simply replacing the electrolyte and recovering the spent material for re-energizing.
(b) Dissolution/deposition- vs. intercalation/de-intercalation-based batteries
Dissolution/deposition-based batteries store energy as a result of chemical reactions occurring inside the cell. The basic mechanism of intercalation/de-intercalation-based batteries is that charged ions (e.g. Li+ or Na+) are shuttled back and forth between the positive and the negative electrodes during charge and discharge processes.
(c) Closed vs. open batteries
A closed battery contains all of the active materials within the cell and the battery is sealed forming a closed system, whereas in an open battery the active material (e.g. oxygen) is not part of the electrodes and is provided from the atmosphere.
On the basis of the electrolyte nature and physical state, batteries are classified as:
(d) Aqueous vs. non-aqueous batteries
The most commonly used aqueous batteries are lead–acid, nickel–cadmium, and nickel–metal hydride batteries that use a water-salt solution as an electrolyte. Non-aqueous batteries use polar-aprotic electrolyte solutions that have no reactive protons or hydrogen atoms. Most Li-ion batteries use lithium salts such as LiPF6, LiAsF6, LiN(SO2CF3)2, or LiSO3CF3 dissolved in solvents such as acetonitrile, ethylene carbonate, diethyl carbonate, propylene carbonate, 1,2-dimethoxyethane, DMSO, THF, or a binary or ternary combination of these to form the electrolyte and are classified as non-aqueous batteries. Non-aqueous electrolytes may be further divided into organic electrolytes, polymeric electrolytes, ionic liquid electrolytes, and solid-state electrolytes.
(e) Solid electrolyte (dry) vs. liquid electrolyte (wet) batteries
Most batteries utilize a liquid as the electrolyte between the two electrodes to regulate the flow of current. However, in some cases, e.g. Li-ion batteries, the liquid electrolyte can lead to safety hazards. Thus, all-solid-state batteries are being developed that have both solid electrodes and solid electrolytes. Solid electrolytes are very good bulk ion conductors but are essentially insulating toward electrons. Examples of such materials include Ag4RbI5 for Ag+ conduction and LiI/Al2O3 mixtures for Li+ conduction. All solid-state batteries can be stacked in a bipolar configuration for better space savings. However, solid-state batteries typically suffer from higher ion-transfer resistance at the electrode–electrolyte interface.
(f) Acidic vs. alkaline batteries
Aqueous batteries can also be classified based on the type of electrolyte used in their construction. There are three common classifications: (i) acidic batteries, such as lead–acid batteries, (ii) near neutral batteries, such as zinc-air batteries, and (iii) alkaline batteries, such as nickel–cadmium and zinc–manganese dioxide batteries.


Thermodynamic principles of batteries. 
Active materials in batteries are usually well-defined, three-dimensional (3D) phases. As such, ideally, active materials have unique chemical potentials, and the Gibbs free energy is used to estimate their equilibrium potential. Thus, their conversion from one oxidation state to another proceeds ideally at a constant potential until almost all of the active materials change from one phase to another. Overall reactions of some well-established battery materials and their performance figures of merit are summarized in Table 2.

Potentials of typical batteries are almost constant in the operating potential window, evidenced with a plateau in charge–discharge profiles. The potential of a cell is a measure of the maximum amount of energy per unit charge that is available to do work when the charge is transferred through an external circuit. (13)Thus, one joule of work is the work done to move one Coulomb of electric charge between two points with a potential difference of one volt. However, if the work flows out of the system, it is assigned a negative sign, whereas the potential of such a system is positive. Therefore: (14)The charge of 1 mole of electrons is a constant called the Faraday constant (F = 96485 C mol−1 e−1). Thus, W = −qE = −nFE(15)where n is the number of electrons transferred per mole. In batteries, via redox reactions, electrons are transferred from one molecule or atom to another. The principles of thermodynamics can be applied to predict the direction of electron flow from one species to another based on the free energy change of the system. Gibb's free energy is defined as the energy available to do useful work in a system. Maximum possible work obtained from a process is equal to the change in Gibbs free energy, ΔG, in the reaction.80 Wmax = ΔG(16)Substituting eqn (16) in eqn (15), we come up with a relationship between Gibbs free energy and cell potential: ΔG = −nFE(17)For a reaction such as aA + bB ⇄ cC + dD(18)at any stage of completion, the change in free energy is given by (19)After dividing both sides by −nF and collecting all the constants together at 25 °C (298 K), we have the Nernst equation that is used to calculate the potential of a cell: (20)The standard free-energy change of formation, ΔG0f, for a reaction can be calculated using the free energies of formation of each component by using the following equation: (21)For example, in lead–acid batteries, the oxidation half-reaction is: Pb(s) + H2SO4(aq) → PbSO4(s) + 2H+(aq) + 2e−(22)and the reduction half-reaction is: PbO2(s) + H2SO4(aq) + 2H+ + 2e− → PbSO4(s) + 2H2O(l)(23)The overall cell reaction is then: Pb(s) + PbO2(s) + 2H2SO4(aq) → 2PbSO4(s) + 2H2O(l)(24)Substituting the appropriate ΔG0f values from the literature85 gives: (25)ΔG0f(rxn) = −371.5 kJWe then calculate the equilibrium cell voltage from (26)the result of which is E0cell = 1.92 V, which applies to aH+; aHSO4−, and aH2O = 1 mole L−1. Electrochemical theory, based on the Nernst equation, eqn (20), can predict how the voltage of the battery changes. Assuming the sulfuric acid in the electrolyte as a strong monoprotic acid and neglecting the dissociation of HSO4−, which is itself a weak acid (Ka = 1.2 × 10−2), the overall reaction of a lead–acid cell can be written as:86 (27)The Nernst equation for this reaction can be expressed as: (28)Since the activity of the pure solid and water equals 1, the equation can be simplified as: (29)Considering the sulfuric acid in batteries as 10 M, Ecell would be 2.04 V, which is equal to the value indicated in Table 2. Thus, the battery delivers nearly constant voltage while discharging, until either or both of the electrodes run out of reactants for their reactions. Thus, near the end of the discharge step, the potential sharply drops to a minimum threshold value. In primary batteries, this means the end of their useful lives; however, secondary batteries can be recharged once they have reached this point. During the recharge process, an external source of energy forces the chemical reactions to reverse by removing electrons from the positive pole and delivering them to the negative pole until the cell is fully recharged.
The charge/discharge rate for batteries is often quoted in a unit called the C-rate. Note that it should not be confused with Coulombs or capacitance that bears the same symbol. A rate of 1C corresponds to the current at which the entire battery would be discharged in one hour. For example, a 1000 mA h battery provides 1000 mA for 1 h if discharged at a 1C rate. If the same battery is discharged at 0.5C it will provide 500 mA for 2 h, likewise at 2C it will deliver 2000 mA for 30 min. Thus a 0.1C (or C/10) discharge rate would take 10 h, and at 10C the discharge process would take 1/10 h. To calculate the C-rate, we have to calculate the theoretical specific capacity of the electrode active material(s). Then we calculate the theoretical specific capacity of the cell based on the amount of the electrode active material used in the cell. This gives us the theoretical current value to charge/discharge the cell at 1 h, i.e. at a 1C rate. Let's consider PbO2 as an example for which the theoretical capacity is 224.1 mA h g−1. If we had one gram and wanted to discharge the PbO2 in one hour, we would put a negative current of 224.1 mA. For an electrode with a mass loading of 15 mg we have: (30)For discharging the above-mentioned battery at a rate of C/5, the current would be 3.36/5 = 0.67 mA h. In the case where the electrode active material is a composite, contributions from all the components should be taken into consideration, so the capacity is calculated as: (31)Since the C-rate is based on the theoretical capacity, the charge/discharge time of the cell would typically be shorter than the one predicted by theory due to a number of kinetic limitations. Note that what the battery manufacturers mean by C-rate is completely different from what scientists mean. In industry, they rate the battery based on the nominal capacity of the battery, not the theoretical capacity.


Advanced electrode materials for batteries. 
As indicated earlier, charge storage in batteries occurs through reversible Faradaic redox reactions throughout the bulk of the electrode active material(s). The amount of electrical energy that a battery delivers is a function of the cell potential that follows certain well-established physico-chemical (thermodynamic and kinetic) principles as explained before. A battery is composed of a negative electrode, a positive electrode, and an electrolyte that allows for ionic conductivity, along with a separator. Thus far, a variety of metal-based species and conductive polymers have been studied to realize advanced technologies in various types of non-lithium ion batteries.87–93 However, Li-ion batteries are considered the best among all battery types because of their superior performance across multiple characteristics. Generally, Li-ion batteries, as state-of-the-art batteries, are based on intercalation and extraction of Li ions for both positive and negative electrodes. When a Li-ion battery is charging, the positive electrode active material gives up some of its Li ions, which flow through the electrolyte to the negative electrode and remain there, thus the energy is stored during this process in the battery. The reverse processes occur when the battery is discharging. The chemical potential of Li in the two electrodes drives Li ions to diffuse out of the negative electrode, thus generating a flow of Li ions from the negative electrode to the positive electrode through the electrolyte. At the same time, to keep the electrodes electrically neutral, the electrons move through an external circuit and travel toward the positive electrode. So, the flow of ions through the electrolyte and the movement of electrons via the external circuit are interconnected processes, and if either stops so does the other. The energy that a battery provides depends on the charge storage capability (Li-ion release and uptake) of the electrode active materials and the redox reactions that take place at the two electrodes.94 Thus, the active material must be designed to allow the easy intercalation/de-intercalation of the reacting species throughout the material.
A number of nice reviews addressing advancements from synthesis to development of the battery electrode active materials have been published.95–98 The positive pole of the Li-ion batteries are typically made of LiMO2 (M = Co, Ni, Mn),99,100 LiMn2O4,101 LiMPO4 (M = Fe, Co, Ni, Mn).102,103 However, the major limitations of LiCoO2 are high cost and capacity fading during deep cycling.96 The main limitation of LiNiO2 is its thermal instability that Ni3+ is readily reduced at high temperatures. LiMnO2 suffers from low cycling performance, because of the conversion of the layered structure of LiMnO2 into a spinel structure during the Li-ion de-intercalation, as well as the leaching of Mn out of the LiMnO2 structure during cycling.96 The major weakness of LiMPO4 compounds is their poor electrical and ionic conductivity or low energy and power density.96 To circumvent these limitations, the pure phases of the electrode active materials are doped with other elements to further improve their energy storage performance. As such, many advanced electrode active materials including LiNi0.5Mn0.5O2,104 LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4,105 LiNixCoyMnzO2 (x + y + z = 1),106,107 LiNi0.5Co0.5PO4,108 Li3V2(PO4)3,109,110 and LiMnxFeyCozPO4 (x + y + z = 1)111,112 are being developed. The choice for the negative electrode active material is almost limited to carbon-based materials96,113 as well as conversion anode materials114 and titanium-based anode materials.115
Lithium transition metal oxides typically have a low intrinsic electronic conductivity and provide a low capacity when produced as micron-sized particles.116 To enhance the energy storage performance of new types of electrode active materials, various strategies have been adopted which are summarized in Fig. 8.96 In one of the most effective approaches, to circumvent these limitations, researchers have explored nanostructured electrode active materials both to increase the effective surface area to boost the charge transfer and to reduce the Li-ion diffusion pathways.98,101,105,117–120
 Fig. 8  General strategies for boosting the energy storage performance of electrode active materials: (a) nanosizing the active materials, (b) compositing the active material, (c) doping and functionalization, (d) tuning morphology of the particles, (e) protecting the material with formation of coatings or shells, and (f) modification of the electrolyte. Reproduced with permission from ref. 96, copyright 2015, Elsevier. 
Despite the remarkable progress made in Li-ion batteries, battery-type energy storage technology still requires extensive further improvement to meet the projected demand. As such, a wide range of post-Li ion materials and systems that could play a pivotal role in meeting such demands are developed for which Choi and Aurbach have provided a nice review.121

Supercapacitors
Capacitance (C) represents the ability of a capacitor to store an electrical charge on its plates. The capacitance of a capacitor can be expressed as: (32)where ε0 is the permittivity or the dielectric constant of air (8.84 × 10−12 F m−1), εr is the permittivity of the dielectric medium used between the two plates, A is the area of the plate in m2, and d denotes the distance between the two plates in m. Thus, the larger and nearer the plates, the higher is the amount of charge stored on a capacitor for the same amount of voltage. Although demonstrating high power capability, the total energy that a physical capacitor stores is very low. It is highly desirable to increase the energy content of an energy storage device without sacrificing its power capability. Nanotechnology provides a popular way to achieve a very high surface area. Reduction of the critical dimensions of energy-storage materials to the nanoscale reduces the diffusion path lengths for ions, and therefore dramatically enhances the surface areas available for capacitive charge storage. The use of an electrolyte with a separator considerably decreases the distance between the plates. Thus, upon cell polarization, the ions of the electrolyte move towards the electrode surface of opposite charge leading to charge separation at the electrode–electrolyte interface in a fast (sub-second to a few seconds timescales) and reversible manner. With such an intriguing approach, the plates of the capacitor are separated with a separator that is exceedingly thin (about just one-molecule-thick compared to dielectric materials in conventional capacitors that might range in thickness from a few microns to a millimeter or more). The resulting capacitor, which is often called a supercapacitor, differs from the conventional capacitors in two important ways: (1) the plates of a supercapacitor have a much bigger surface area, and (2) the distance between the plates is much smaller. As a result, the charge that a supercapacitor can store is thousands, millions, or even billions of times higher than that of an ordinary capacitor. Typical capacitors are usually rated in the pF, nF, and μF range, whereas commercial supercapacitors such as the ones made by Maxwell Technologies® have capacitances rated up to several thousand Farads.122 Please note that the electric double layer is formed on both the positive and negative electrodes, thus a supercapacitor cell actually comprises two capacitors connected in series.
All the methods to test supercapacitors are based on the assumption that, in certain cases, the equivalent circuit of the supercapacitors can be simplified as a resistor–capacitor (R–C) circuit123 (please see the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy section for more explanation on the equivalent circuits of the energy storage devices). Thus, we consider a system consisting of a resistor and an ideal capacitor in series to demonstrate the physical principle of charge storage in supercapacitors (Fig. 9). Such a system can be charged with either a constant current or a constant voltage, though in practical applications, constant current charging–discharging a supercapacitor is more common.

Constant current charging–discharging an R–C circuit. 
With constant current charging an R–C circuit, the potential of the system, across the plates of the capacitor, VC, changes with time (Fig. 10).

Mechanisms of charge storage in supercapacitors
As indicated before, the charge/discharge processes in supercapacitive active materials are fundamentally different from those in batteries. Charge storage processes in supercapacitors are, ideally, 2-D and proceed via two main mechanisms: (i) accumulation of static charge within the double layer (that results in EDLC), and (ii) charge storage based on Faradaic redox reactions on the electrode surfaces.125,126
Electrical double layer capacitance
The energy storage mechanism in EDLCs is based on the electrostatic adsorption of the electrolyte ions on the surface of the electrode active materials. Thus, the key to the high capacitance of EDLCs is in using a highly accessible surface area and electronically conducting electrode materials. Nanostructured carbon-based materials with a high specific surface area and good electrical conductivity are the materials of choice for EDLCs. Carbon-based materials3,127,128 including graphene derivatives,34,129–134 activated carbon,128,135 carbon nanotubes,136–138 carbon nanohorns,139–142 carbon nanocoils,143,144 carbon onions,145,146 carbon fibers,147–149 carbon fabrics,150–152 carbon cloth,153,154 carbon aerogels,128,155,156 carbide-derived carbons,67,157,158 zeolite-templated carbons,159,160 and porous carbons161–164 with electrical conductivity, electrochemical stability, and highly accessible surface area as well as low cost satisfy all the requirements for this application. The double layer capacitance of the carbon-based materials is governed by their structure, especially the porosity and specific surface area.
Simon and Gogotsi experimentally investigated the relationship between the capacitance and the pore size in solvent-free ionic liquid electrolytes.165,166 Their studies reveal that the maximum capacitance can be achieved when the pore size optimally matches the ion size. Ji et al. analyzed the relationship between the capacitance and the specific surface area for various carbon-based electrodes.33 The gravimetric capacitance (F g−1) increases with an increase in the specific surface area of the carbon electrode, but the true area normalized capacitance, which is the ratio of the specific capacitance to the specific surface area, gradually decreases and finally stabilizes at about 5 μF cm−2 when the specific surface area exceeds 1500 m2 g−1. However, we have made a breakthrough in this direction by laser reduction of graphene oxide to grow and pattern reduced graphene oxide on flexible substrates70,71 (Fig. 14). The approach effectively prevents the aggregation of graphene sheets and enables the laser-scribed graphene to feature a high specific surface area (1520 m2 g−1) and a significantly high electrical conductivity (1738 S m−1), thereby exhibiting a high geometric area normalized capacitance of up to 4.04 mF cm−2 at a specific current of 1 A g−1. Thus, the carbon-based materials, made with fine pore size and controlled specific surface area, enable optimized ionic diffusion and high capacitance.
 Fig. 14  (a–f) Schematic illustration of the fabrication of laser-scribed graphene-based supercapacitors and (g–i) micro-supercapacitors. Reproduced with permission from ref. 70, copyright 2012, American Association for the Advancement of Science. (j and k) Fabrication of more than 100 micro-supercapacitors can be realized in a single run; the micro-supercapacitors are completely flexible. Reproduced with permission from ref. 71, copyright 2013, Springer Nature. 
Gravimetric double layer capacitance of carbon-based materials can exceed 300 F g−1 at 1 A g−1 in aqueous electrolytes,167–169 but barely approaches 300 F g−1 in organic electrolytes.70,170–173 Discovery and development of heteroatom-doped carbon-based materials has become a fast-growing branch of electrode active materials and provides an important alternative to pristine carbon-based materials. The doping of heteroatoms such as nitrogen (N), oxygen (O), boron (B), sulfur (S) and phosphorus (P) into the lattice of carbon-based materials leads to enhanced chemical activity, improved wettability, increased conductivity and can also boost pseudocapacitive characteristics.174,175 B and N as neighbors to C in the periodic table have a rather similar atomic size, but one electron less (B) or more (N), which makes them appropriate candidates for heteroatom doping. Doping with N plays a more critical role in modifying the carbon frameworks, because N forms strong covalent bonds with carbon atoms and imposes electronic conductivity and serves as active sites that ultimately leads to a superior energy storage performance.140,144,176,177
Generally, EDLCs offer great power performance and cycle life and are characterized by nearly rectangular CVs and triangular GCD profiles. The rectangular-shaped CV for EDLCs stems from the fundamental nature of the double-layer charge storage mechanism. In EDLCs, adsorption and desorption of ions at the electrode–electrolyte interface, in the absence of diffusion limitations, is almost instantaneous, so that dV/dt is a constant.15 The first and simplest EDL model is the Helmholtz model. Based on this model, under an applied cell voltage, an electrode immersed in an electrolyte attracts counter-ions, while repelling co-ions. Thus, two compact layers of charges form at the electrode–electrolyte interfaces, which are called the “electric double layer”. The double layer capacitance, Cdl, at each electrode–electrolyte interface resembles that of a conventional parallel plate capacitor, eqn (32). In contrast to the Helmholtz assumption, the charged ions are mobile in the electrolyte solutions and are under the influence of both diffusion and electrostatic forces. Consequently, the Gouy–Chapman model was developed, in which the ions are treated as point charges and the concentration of the ions in the solution near the surface follows a Boltzmann distribution. Although this makes a better approach to reality, the Gouy–Chapman model fails for highly charged double layers.178 The most sophisticated post-Helmholtz EDL model is the Gouy–Chapman–Stern model, which combines the Helmholtz and the Gouy–Chapman ideas (Fig. 15). On this account, the Gouy–Chapman–Stern model more accurately describes the detailed structure of the electric double layer.179 Based on the Gouy–Chapman–Stern model, the EDL has, in fact, a multi-layer structure, including the inner Helmholtz plane (IHP), outer Helmholtz plane (OHP), and a diffuse layer that is next to the bulk electrolyte solution. In the Stern layer, which comprises the IHP and OHP, counter-ions are unsolvated or partially solvated due to the strong interactions with the ions and the electrode surfaces and pores. It is worth mentioning that the potential decays linearly from the surface potential to both the inner and the outer compact layers, but with different gradients or slopes. Approaching the diffuse layer, the change in potential deviates from linearity and reaches a plateau and remains almost constant through the bulk of the electrolyte. Thus, the total double layer capacitance, Cdl, of the electrode can be expressed as: (61)in which CStern is the double layer capacitance of the Stern layer, and CDiff represents the double layer capacitance of the diffuse region.
 Fig. 15  Double layer models. The Gouy–Chapman–Stern electric double-layer model of a planar surface. IHP and OHP stand for the inner Helmholtz plane and outer Helmholtz plane, respectively. A Stern layer and a diffuse layer developed in the electrolyte due to the thermal motions of electrolyte ions are represented. The scheme also shows the potential gradient across the double layer. The inset circuit shows the in-series capacitors of the Stern and diffuse layers. 
In recent years, porous structures of electrode active materials, which provide a very high specific surface area and well-defined pathways to electrolyte access, have drawn considerable attention. Although the above-mentioned models provide a satisfactory description of the EDL on plane surfaces, they fail to appropriately describe charge distribution in porous materials. Movement of ions in the bulk electrolyte is completely different from that within the pores of the electrode active material. Pore size has a great influence on the mobility of ions inside the pores. An appropriate pore size substantially enhances the capacitance of carbon-based materials. The highest capacitances could be obtained when the pore size matches with the radius of the solvated ions in the electrolyte (Fig. 16).180
 Fig. 16  Schematic representation of the anomalous increase in capacitance of porous carbon-based materials because of the ion-sized pores. 
The pores with a diameter less than 0.5 nm are not accessible to hydrated ions, and those with a diameter less than 1.0 nm are too small to be accessible to organic electrolytes, thus, based on the Gouy–Chapman–Stern model, they do not contribute to the EDLC. Among mesoporous materials, electrode active materials with small mesoporosity (about 3 to 5 nm in diameter) demonstrate the best capacitive performance, since the diameter of the pores is about two times that of the radius of the solvated ions to allow for proper ion adsorption on both adjacent pore walls.181 However, later on, Gogotsi's group teamed up with Simon and reported an anomalous increase in capacitance at pores smaller than the size of the solvated electrolyte ions (Fig. 17).180 Although the whole mechanism is still not completely understood, the increase in capacitance of the microporous materials can be ascribed to the partial desolvation of the ions when they enter the sub-nano-size pores of carbon.182,183 With the distortion of the solvation shell and decrease in distance between the ion and carbon surfaces, the capacitance of the system increases based on eqn (32).
 Fig. 17  (A) A plot of areal capacitance normalized by BET specific surface area vs. average pore size for different carbon samples. All the samples were tested in the same electrolyte (NEt4BF4 in acetonitrile). The traditional view is based on an assumption that the normalized capacitance continually decreases with decreasing pore size. The results presented in this figure show that the normalized capacitance decreases with decreasing pore size until a critical value is reached after which a huge increase in the capacitance is observed. (B–D) Schematic representation of solvated ions residing in pores with the distance between adjacent pore walls (B) greater than 2 nm, (C) between 1 and 2 nm, and (D) less than 1 nm. Reproduced with permission from ref. 180, copyright 2006, American Association for the Advancement of Science. 
Moving beyond the planar capacitor model, the influence of pore curvature on the capacitance of the porous carbon materials should be taken into consideration. There have been tremendous attempts to properly formulate the capacitance of porous carbon materials based on the shape and size of the pores.184 Pores of carbon materials have various shapes including cylindrical, spherical and slit types, depending on the synthesis approach. Assuming that the mesopores are cylindrical, the capacitance of the system is given by: (62)in which L is the pore length and b and a are the radii of the outer and inner cylinders, respectively (Fig. 18I). Since the area of a cylinder is 2πbL, the normalized double-cylinder capacitance can be expressed as: (63)But in micropores, the ions line up in the center of a cylindrical pore, thus adsorption of the ions can be described by an ‘electrical wire-in-cylinder’ model (Fig. 18II) and the capacitance is given by:181,184 (64)where a0 is the effective (fully desolvated) size of the ion. With eqn (64), Gogotsi and Simon were able to explain the reason behind an anomalous increase in capacitance with decreasing pore size to less than 1 nm.
 Fig. 18  Schematic diagrams (top views) of (I) a negatively charged mesopore with solvated cations approaching the pore wall to form an electric double-cylinder capacitor, where a is the radius of the inner cylinder, b is the pore radius, and d is the distance of approach of the cation to the pore wall. (II) A negatively charged micropore of radius b with the cation size of a0 that forms an electric wire-in-cylinder capacitor. (III) Cross-section of an exohedral capacitor with the inner and outer sphere/cylinder radii of a and b, respectively, separated by the effective double-layer thickness d. Panels I and II are adapted with permission from ref. 185, copyright 2008, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH and Co., and panel III is adapted with permission from ref. 184, copyright 2011, Cambridge University Press. 
In reality, the pores of the electrode active materials are not unimodal, instead they comprise multimodal pores in the range from sub-nanometer to a few nanometers. Thus, one should take the contributions to capacitance from macro-, meso-, and micropores into consideration, that is:185 (65)In addition to porous carbon-based materials, other carbon materials have also been extensively studied as electrode active materials for supercapacitors. However, in sharp contrast to porous carbon-based materials that have a negative surface curvature where the counter-ions can enter the pores to form endohedral supercapacitors, for zero-dimensional (0D) fullerenes and carbon nano-onions (i.e. multilayer fullerenes), and 1D end-capped single-/multi-walled carbon nanotubes as well as carbon nanowires, the materials have a positive surface curvature and the counter-ions can only reside on the outer surfaces, leading to exohedral supercapacitors (Fig. 18III). The capacitance of an exohedral electric double-sphere capacitor is given as:184 (66)For which the normalized capacitance is: (67)The normalized capacitance of an exohedral double-cylinder capacitor is given as:184 (68)In these equations, a is the radius of the inner sphere/cylinder, b the radius of the outer sphere/cylinder, and d the effective double-layer thickness. Graphene-based materials with near-zero curvature do not belong to either of the above-mentioned categories, instead they reside in between and can be treated with the classical EDLC approach.
EDLC proceeds simultaneously on both the positive and negative electrodes. Thus, each electrode–electrolyte interface represents a capacitor, and the whole cell can be considered as two capacitors in series: (69)where Cp and Cn are the capacitance of the positive and negative electrodes, respectively.
Pseudocapacitance
In spite of the outstanding improvements in properties of carbon-based materials, their limited energy generally restricts EDLC-based supercapacitors to applications requiring power delivery over only a few seconds at a time. Consequently, many research efforts are focused on designing other electrode active materials to overcome the challenge of the low energy of supercapacitors. Supercapacitors with higher energy densities can be achieved by using pseudocapacitive materials. RuO2 was the first material to demonstrate pseudocapacitance.64 RuO2 has a wide potential window (1.2 V), high proton conductivity, good thermal stability, and metallic-type conductivity. In an acidic medium, the highly reversible redox behavior of RuO2 is as follows: RuO2 + δH+ + δe− → RuO2−δ(OH)δ(70)where the Ru oxidation states can change from (II) up to (IV) (0 ≤ δ ≤ 2). However, the high cost of the ruthenium-based materials has limited their applications to a few high value electronic devices.
Manganese oxides with a relatively low cost, low toxicity, environmental safety, and theoretical high capacitance of up to 1300 F g−1 seem to be an appropriate alternative to RuO2. The general form of the Faradaic reaction of manganese oxides is (Fig. 19a):3 MnIVO2 + xH+ + yM+ + (x + y)e− → MnIII(x+y)MnIV1−(x+y)OOMyHx (0 ≤ x + y ≤ 1)(71)where M+ can be H+, Li+, Na+, or K+ and the redox reaction involves transitions between different oxidation states.
 Fig. 19  (a) Schematic CV for a MnO2 electrode in a mild aqueous electrolyte (0.1 M K2SO4) that shows the successive multiple surface redox reactions leading to the pseudocapacitive charge storage mechanism. The red (upper) part is related to the oxidation from Mn(III) to Mn(IV) and the blue (lower) part refers to the reduction from Mn(IV) to Mn(III). (b) Schematic representation of different charge-storage mechanisms of MnO2 as a function of operating potential window; B: battery behavior; PC: pseudocapacitance; (IV), (III), and (II): Mn ions in the oxidation states of 4, 3, and 2, respectively. The red and blue lines are the dq/dV plots of the Mn–Si–O composite electrode obtained at scan rates of 2 and 100 mV s−1, respectively. Panel a is adapted with permission from ref. 3, copyright 2008, Springer Nature, and panel b is adapted with permission from ref. 186, copyright 2015, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH and Co. with slight modifications. 
It should be noted that although RuO2 and MnO2 are considered as pseudocapacitive metal oxides, upon changing the experimental conditions, redox peaks can appear in their CV curves. For example, the CV of MnO2 recorded in the potential range from −1.0 to 1.0 V (vs. Ag/AgCl) displays a clear set of redox peaks (Fig. 19b), whereas the voltammogram in the potential range from 0 to 0.9 V is quite rectangular. This observation implies that pseudocapacitance likely originates from a unique state of the metal oxides. From Fig. 19b it can also be deduced that the overall pseudocapacitive characteristics of MnO2 over the entire 2 V range is estimated to involve 0.65 e− per MnO2, which accounts for nearly 40% of the total charge-storage capacity of MnO2 at a scan rate of 2 mV s−1.
Materials used for building pseudocapacitors include various transition metal oxides/nitrides/phosphides/sulfides, as well as conducting polymers.187 Metal oxides188,189 such as RuO2,64,190–197 MnO2,198–201 cobalt oxides (CoO, CoOOH, Co2O3, Co3O4),202–209 NiO,210–216 CuO,217–226 iron oxides (FeOOH, Fe2O3, and Fe3O4),227–232 and V2O5,233 binary metal oxides234 such as AB2O4 (A = Ni, Co, Mn, Cu or Zn B = Mn, Co or Fe),235,236 CoMoO4, and NiMnO3,237 metal nitrides,238,239 such as VN, RuN, TiN, WN, Ni3N, NbN, Ta3N5, and Co3Mo3N, metal phosphides240 such as amorphous Ni–P,241 Ni2P, Ni5P4, and Co2P, metal sulfides such as FeCo2S4,242 NiCo2S4,242,243 NiS2, CoS2244 and the binary NixCo1−xS2,245 MxSy (M = Cu, Ag, Au),246 Ni3S2@MoS2,247 MxCo3−xS4 (M = Ni, Mn, Zn),248 CuCo2S4249 and CoCu2S4250 as well as electronically conducting polymers251–257 including polyaniline and polypyrrole have extensively been studied as pseudocapacitive materials. Note that, based on the criteria presented here, the reported values of capacitance and the metrics for evaluating the energy storage processes that are truly involved in the above-mentioned studies may need to be revisited. Besides metal oxides and conducting polymers that have long been sought for pseudocapacitive energy storage, 2D carbides and nitrides (known as MXenes),258–262 metal–organic frameworks (MOFs),263–269 and layered double hydroxides (LDHs)270–275 are the latest promising pseudocapacitive materials, and are now undergoing intensive investigations. One of the most efficient and attractive ways to boost pseudocapacitive performance of the materials is conjugation of the (mostly planar) organic/inorganic redox active species to carbon-based materials (via covalent, non-covalent, or π–π stacking interactions), in which the former gives a significant boost to energy performance of the system and the latter guarantees its high power capability.276–281
Charge storage mechanisms in a pseudocapacitive electrode can be classified as (1) redox pseudocapacitance, (2) intercalation pseudocapacitance, (3) doping pseudocapacitance, and (4) underpotential deposition based pseudocapacitance (Fig. 20).
 Fig. 20  Different types of redox mechanisms that give rise to pseudocapacitance. (a) Redox pseudocapacitance, as in RuO2·xH2O. (b) Intercalation pseudocapacitance, as in Nb2O5. (c) Doping pseudocapacitance, as in polypyrrole and (d) underpotential deposition based pseudocapacitance, as in the deposition of lead on the surface of a gold electrode. CVs of panels a, b, c, and d are reproduced with permission from ref. 282, copyright 2002, American Chemical Society; ref. 283, copyright 2012, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH and Co.; ref. 284, copyright 2006, Elsevier; and ref. 285, copyright 2014, Multidisciplinary Digital Publishing Institute, respectively. 
Charge storage in pseudocapacitive materials involves redox reactions, thus is Faradaic in nature but capacitive in behavior. However, not all Faradaic processes contribute to pseudocapacitive charge storage.14 Although both battery-type and pseudocapacitive electrodes involve Faradaic redox reactions, the kinetics and electrochemical profiles of the two processes are completely different. The relation between the current and potential scan rate can be generally expressed as:286,287 i = aνb(72)with the value of b providing insight into the mechanism of charge storage. In pseudocapacitive electrodes, the current changes linearly with the sweep rate (i.e. b = 1) according to:10 |i| = ACυ(73)where A is the surface area of the active materials and C represents the capacitance, whereas in a typical battery the peak current of a reversible redox process is proportional to the square root of the sweep rate (i.e. b = 0.5), according to:288 (74)in which C* is the concentration of the species (mol cm−3), D is the diffusion coefficient (cm2 s−1), the χ function at any given point on the wave is a pure number, and the other symbols have their usual meaning. Thus, the relationship can be simply expressed as: (75)Thus, the kinetics of pseudocapacitance resembles an adsorption-controlled electrochemical process (i.e. i ∼ ν), which is opposed to a diffusion-controlled redox process (i.e. i ∼ ν1/2) governing the electrochemical response of battery-type electrodes. As such, the kinetic information obtained from CV can be used to differentiate between batteries and pseudocapacitors. The power of the sweep rate in the aforementioned relationship for hybrid systems is in between (i.e., i ∼ ν0.5<b<1).
In redox pseudocapacitors, the charge is stored through Faradaic charge-transfer reactions when ions are electrochemically adsorbed onto the surface or near the surface of a material. Intercalation pseudocapacitance occurs when ions (e.g. Li+, K+, Na+, or H+) are reversibly inserted into and extracted from the tunnels or layers of a rigid and layered host material, which is accompanied by a Faradaic charge-transfer, but not phase transformations. This mechanism of charge storage makes use of the advantages of batteries in storing the charge in the bulk of the active material, yet the process is not limited by the diffusion of cations within the crystalline lattice of the electrode active materials. However, intercalation pseudocapacitance is rarely observed, and most of these materials exhibit a hybrid charge storage mechanism. For example, the current response of orthorhombic Nb2O5 can be described as a combination of two separate mechanisms, namely capacitive behavior, and battery-like character.287 For sweep rates ranging from 0.1 to 20 mV s−1, the b-value, eqn (72), for both the cathodic and anodic peaks is 1, demonstrating a fast kinetic and capacitive response. However, at sweep rates >50 mV s−1 the b-value decreases to 0.8 and 0.7 for the cathodic and anodic currents, respectively, indicating considerable battery-like behavior. It is possible to discriminate between contributions from intercalative pseudocapacitance and a battery-type energy storage mechanism, the details of which are provided in the Resolving the capacitor-like and battery-like behavior section.
Doping pseudocapacitance involves reversible electrochemical doping and dedoping in conductive polymers. Underpotential deposition usually takes place when metal ions or hydrogen form an adsorbed monolayer at a surface of a noble metal well above their redox potential. With underpotential deposition of hydrogen on platinum, eqn (76), a monolayer of H atoms covers a Pt surface in which the electrosorption/electrodesorption processes are associated with charge storage. Pt + H+ + e− ↔ Pt–Hads(76)For this 2D surface process, an electrochemical adsorption/desorption isotherm can be written. Assuming no lateral interactions and a random distribution of the H atoms over the Pt surface, the electrosorption process can be explained by the Langmuir adsorption isotherm. Thus, a Nernst-like relationship for the above-mentioned process can be written: (77)in which θPt–H is the surface occupied by the H atoms (0 < θPt–H < 1). Pseudocapacitance of the system can be expressed as: (78) (79)where q0Pt–H is the surface charge required for completion of a monolayer of H atoms on Pt. Two important points arise from these expressions: (1) there is a continuous range of potentials, due to the continuous possible range of θPt–H values from 0 to ∼1, and (2) the maximum value of CPC appears at θPt–H = 0.5, thus: (80)Therefore, the discharge process in a supercapacitor is associated with a continuing linear decline of voltage, whereas the discharge process of well-defined battery materials takes place at a nearly constant voltage until near the end of the discharge step.
The common CV feature in these four mechanisms is that the current is proportional to the scan rate and that the shape of the CVs is near rectangular and if peaks are present, they are broad with a small peak-to-peak separation. The Faradaic character of the so-defined pseudocapacitive processes is given by a Nernst-like equation, eqn (81), summarized in Table 3. (81)In addition to the linear proportionality of i–ν, high coulombic efficiency and fast kinetics (highly reversibly CVs) are indicative of a pseudocapacitive behavior. Thus, an electrode material with well-separated redox peaks in the CV and a discharge curve with a plateau in the GCD profile should not be considered a supercapacitor.
