Pseudocapacitive materials typically exhibit a higher capacitance and energy compared to EDLCs. In general, the specific capacitance of a pseudocapacitive material is 1–2 orders of magnitude greater than that of EDLC processes (Table 1). However, pseudocapacitive processes often demonstrate a low power performance and suffer from lack of long cycle life and mechanical stability. This is because pseudocapacitive processes generally involve metal oxides with poor electrical conductivity and sluggish ion diffusion,8,289 or conducting polymers that swell, shrink and crack during doping/de-doping of charged ions and degrade at relatively high over-potentials.290,291 Thus, while individual EDLC and pseudocapacitive materials show some of the required properties, none of them can offer all the desirable properties to achieve the desired energy content, high-power performance and long cycle life.
Merging the best properties of each of the components into a composite design or hybrid architecture opens up opportunities to develop electrode active materials that possess the advantages of the individual building blocks, while eliminating the associated shortcomings. This appealing approach may also allow synergistic activities between the components that are unattainable by individual components.235,292–297 Thus, huge efforts have been devoted worldwide by researchers to fabricate composite electrode materials with enhanced energy storage performance. To date, the composite electrode materials that have been introduced to promote energy storage efficiency of the systems mainly include composites of transition metal dichalcogenides with conducting polymers,298 composites of MXenes with conducting polymers, carbon-based materials, and metal oxides,299,300 composites of metal oxides (including RuO2, MnO2, V2O5, Fe3O4, Co3O4, NiO, CuO and TiO2) with carbon-based materials,188,226,234,301–304 composites of organic electroactive molecules (including quinone and its derivatives) with activated carbon301 and conducting polymers,294,305 composites of conducting polymers with carbon-based materials,306–308 and metal oxides,188,303,307 composites of layered double hydroxide (LDH) with carbon-based materials and conducting polymers,271 composites of transition metal nitrides, bimetal oxides, and hydroxides,188,237,309,310 as well as lithium metal species,304,311 and a variety of graphene-containing nanocomposites.134,312–315
Hybrid energy storage systems
An ideal energy storage device is one that exhibits both high energy and high power. In spite of significant improvements in increasing the energy content of supercapacitors, their energy is still low. Hybrid energy storage systems aim to accomplish comparable performance to batteries in energy content, and to maintain the high power characteristic and good cycle life of supercapacitors. A hybrid energy storage system can be achieved by hybridization at either the device or the materials level, which is also known as external and internal hybrids, respectively.75
At the device level, an EDLC and a battery can be integrated in tandem in modules. In addition to an EDLC and a battery, a device level hybrid energy storage system requires power conversion electronics to independently manage the two energy storage devices. Because of these requirements, device level hybrid systems are multicomponent and typically suffer from manufacturing complexity, higher cost, and higher weight or volume. Current research on energy storage aims to accomplish battery level energy and supercapacitor level power and cyclability in a single device. Materials level hybridization is a step forward to achieve something better than the externally hybridized systems. In materials level hybridization, a hybrid system involves one battery-like charge storage process and one capacitive (either EDLC-type or pseudocapacitive) charge storage process (Fig. 21). For example, we have previously reported hybridization of an asymmetric supercapacitor based on activated carbon (which can be used alone as a supercapacitor electrode material) with zinc (which is an electrode active material for batteries) in a parallel combination.76 In the fabricated hybrid energy storage system, activated carbon is the negative electrode of the asymmetric supercapacitor, Zn serves as the negative electrode of the secondary battery, and self-doped polyaniline performs as a shared positive electrode. Since the operating potential window of the supercapacitive material matches that of the battery material, the hybrid system benefits from the high-energy advantages of the battery and the high-power capabilities of the supercapacitor.
 Fig. 21  Hybrid energy storage systems. (a) Schematic representation of the energy storage mechanism and structure of a battery–supercapacitor hybrid (BSH) device. (b) Various types of battery–supercapacitor hybrid devices. Reproduced with permission from ref. 73, copyright 2017, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH and Co. 
More recently Ding, Mitlin, and coworkers have presented a nice review of hybrid ion capacitors, which are a relatively new class of hybrid devices.316 Hybrid ion capacitors are intermediate in energy between batteries and supercapacitors, while, in principle, demonstrating supercapacitor-like power performance and cyclability.

Capacitance from redox electrolytes. 
As already indicated, the main charge storage mechanism to increase the energy content of supercapacitors beyond electric double layer capacitance is the incorporation of solid-state redox-active electrode materials that employ fast Faradaic redox reactions for charge storage (known as pseudocapacitive materials). Inspired from pseudocapacitive charge storage processes, hybrid energy storage systems can utilize liquid-state redox-active electrolytes to supply extra-capacity. Note that redox electrolytes contribute to charge storage via Faradaic non-capacitive processes, thus, are not part of supercapacitors. Redox electrolytes have made substantial progress as a means to boost Faradaic charge storage in hybrid systems, and several excellent reviews on redox electrolytes can be found elsewhere.78,317,318 Redox electrolytes are classified under two main categories; (a) redox-active electrolytes which are inherently able to undergo redox reactions, and (b) redox-additive electrolytes in which redox active species are introduced into the electrolyte to enable fast Faradaic reactions at the electrode/electrolyte interface.78 The utilization of redox electrolytes has several advantages:317 (1) the approach is readily scalable because redox active species are simply dissolved into the existing electrolyte. (2) Synthesis of solid-state electrode active materials, some of which involve complicated multi-step processes, can be avoided. Instead, simple electrodes, such as commercially available carbon-based materials, can be used. (3) High-power performance is maintained because the redox reactions are not limited by the kinetics of solid-state diffusion. (4) The redox potential of the system, consequently, the sloping plateaus of the discharge profile of the device, can be tuned by the design of custom organic redox-active molecules. (5) The dead weight of the electrochemically inert electrolyte of the conventional energy storage systems is utilized as an active component to augment energy storage performance of the device. To date, different classes of redox additives have been introduced including halides (especially iodide and bromide),319,320 ionic liquids,321 transition metal cations (Cu, V, Fe, etc.),78 inorganic and organometallic complexes (such as metal bipyridine complexes, ferro/ferricyanide, etc.),322–324 and organic ring compounds (viologens, dihydroxybenzenes, quinone derivatives, etc.).27,32,79,294 Redox additives should have (i) high stability, (ii) reversible redox reactions, (iii) chemical and electrochemical stability in the investigated voltage window, (iv) compatibility with other cell components, and ideally (v) a standard redox potential close to the oxygen evolution potential for the positive electrode electrolyte and near the hydrogen evolution potential for the negative electrode electrolyte. The latter characteristic shifts the voltage plateau of the hybrid device to a higher voltage value, resulting in a huge increase in energy content of the device.
We have recently introduced another paradigm into the subdomain of Faradaic charge storage based on redox electrolytes, that is catalytic interactions between electrode active materials and solution-phase redox species.27 Thus, charge storage mechanisms can be generally categorized as: qtot = qNon-Faradaic + qFaradaic = (qElectric + qElectrolytic) + (qSurface + qInterface + qSurf.–Interf.interaction)(82)In this relation, qNon-Faradaic is composed of contributions from electrical charge storage (as in physical capacitors and EDLCs). qFaradaic is composed of contributions from surface reactions of the immobilized active materials (as in batteries and pseudocapacitors), and reactions of redox electrolytes at the interface (as in flow batteries), along with the q provided via the interactions between electrode active materials and solution-phase redox species.
A summary of some reported redox additives and their corresponding redox potentials is presented in Fig. 22. It should be noted that although the requirements for supercapacitors, hybrid systems and batteries are substantially different, redox active electrolytes are a common concept involved in the description of the energy storage mechanism of all the devices, especially hybrid systems and flow batteries. However, to truly realize the promise of redox-active electrolytes, hybrid systems must simultaneously utilize two independent redox couples, one for the positive electrode electrolyte and the other one for the negative electrode electrolyte.317 When just a single redox couple is included in the electrolyte, the potential of the EDLC electrode will reach the edge of the electrolyte voltage stability window, while the electrode with its redox couple will not yet have been fully charged. Mass balancing the electrode active materials on the two electrodes can somewhat alleviate this problem. However, the increased device mass or volume will decrease the mass-/volume-normalized energy of the system (Fig. 23).
 Fig. 22  The redox potential of representative redox-active species with potential for hybrid energy storage systems, redox batteries, as well as flow batteries. (a) Redox potential and the demonstrated volumetric capacity for some aqueous redox couples. The suitable pH ranges for each species is also demonstrated. The redox-active electrolytes illustrated in red, violet, and blue operate under acidic, neutral, or basic conditions, respectively. Potential of various inorganic (b), and organic & organometallic (c) redox couples. Chemical abbreviations: DHAQ, 2,6-dihydroxyanthraquinone; AQDS, 9,10-anthraquinone-2,7-disulfonic acid; EV, ethyl viologen; MV, methyl viologen; PV, pentyl viologen; HV, heptyl viologen; TEMPO, (2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidin-1-yl)oxyl [(–OH), 4-hydroxy-TEMPO; (–SO4), TEMPO-4-sulfate; FcNCl, (ferrocenylmethyl) trimethylammonium chloride; (–NMe3), 4-trimethylammonium-TEMPO]; TCNQ, tetracyanoquinodimethane; AQ, 9,10-anthraquinone; BQ, 1,4-benzoquinone.  Fig. 23  Balancing the capacity of a redox EC electrode (“redox EC” stands for the redox-enhanced electrochemical capacitor that we refer to as a class of hybrid energy storage systems). (a) Galvanostatic charging voltage profiles for a single redox couple EC electrode with an EDLC counter electrode of equal mass, an EDLC counter electrode with four times higher mass (left), and a dual-redox EC (right). (b) GCD profiles of the devices with different combinations. The energy stored/recovered by each combination is proportional to the area under the charge/discharge profiles. (c) The total and normalized (gravimetric or volumetric) energy stored by systems are presented here. Reproduced with permission from ref. 317, copyright 2017, American Chemical Society. 
To achieve an understanding of the charge/discharge profile of a hybrid energy storage system, it's necessary to realize what the charge/discharge behaviors of a typical battery and supercapacitor look like. The voltage provided by a battery drops quickly at the beginning of the discharge. After this period, the voltage remains relatively constant (which is also called the discharge voltage plateau) until the battery is near complete exhaustion, then the voltage drops off sharply. Since batteries deliver a relatively constant operating voltage over an extended period of time, it is often difficult to precisely determine the state-of-charge of a battery. Whereas batteries deliver a steady voltage in the vast majority of their whole discharge time (∼95%), the voltage output of a supercapacitor, especially EDLCs, decreases nearly linearly as current flows, thus preventing the use of its full energy spectrum (Fig. 24).
 Fig. 24  Charge–discharge profiles of a battery, an EDLC, and a hybrid device. The area under the discharge profile of the devices reflects their energy levels. Note that charge–discharge profiles of the pseudocapacitors resemble that of a hybrid energy storage device. 
From a practical point of view, this means that while there still remains a lot of energy in a supercapacitor, a connected device will not work after a certain voltage drop. Since the operating voltage of supercapacitors changes linearly with time during the charge/discharge process, the state-of-charge of a supercapacitor can be precisely measured. Fig. 24 clearly shows the differences between the energy of a battery and a supercapacitor. The discharge profile of the battery exhibits a fixed voltage line. The area under the discharge line, qV, shows the energy of the battery. The voltage across a capacitor device that is charged to a total charge of q varies linearly with the stored charge. Thus, the total energy that a capacitor stores equals the area under the straight line of the EDLC, i.e. that can also be expressed as . Therefore, to charge a battery and a supercapacitor to a terminal charge of q with a potential of V, the charge that is stored with a supercapacitor is half that of a battery. It should be noted that the charge and discharge profiles of an EDLC are nearly superimposed, confirming that EDLC-based processes, by nature, are highly reversible, thus their coulombic efficiency approaches 100%. Considering the charge–discharge profiles of the energy storage devices presented in Fig. 24:
To be “high power” the time of discharge should not be increased.
To be “high energy” the area under the q–V profile should be increased.
The current of charging/discharging is also constant.
Thus, to achieve higher energy in a hybrid system, the charge–discharge profile inevitably deviates from linearity and approaches that of battery-like behavior (Fig. 24). The steadier output voltage allows hybrid systems to provide more constant power over an extended period of a discharge step, which is a significant advantage over EDLCs. Consequently, a much bigger fraction of the total energy can be utilized, compared to EDLCs for which the unusable fraction of the energy reaches about 25%.317,326 Note that in EDLC systems the time-voltage response is linear, therefore, under a constant current condition the power decreases steadily with time. As the voltage is reduced, maintaining constant power draws more and more current. Once an EDLC has discharged below 50% of its maximum voltage, because of the increase in the IR drop and limitations of power electronics, the remaining energy that accounts for 25% of the total energy is essentially unusable (the area of a right triangle with height h/2 is 1/4th of the triangle with height h).
Considering the V–q relationship of batteries and conventional capacitors, along with non-idealities i.e. convex or concave slope GCD profiles that are associated with supercapacitors and hybrid systems, the amount of energy that an energy storage device of any type stores can be generally represented as: E = nqV; 0 < n ≤ 1(83)As indicated in Fig. 25, charge–discharge profiles of a hybrid energy storage system can deviate considerably from the one that is expected from an ideal capacitor. Thus, the use of  does not hold anymore, because it could result in either an overestimation or an underestimation of the energy that a supercapacitor stores/delivers.14
 Fig. 25  Charge–discharge profiles of two supercapacitors that deviate considerably from an ideal capacitive behavior. 
In charge storage based on the EDLC mechanism, the active materials will not be decomposed, thus the device can survive up to a million charge/discharge cycles. At high rates of charge and discharge, where redox reactions cannot proceed to any great extent, EDLC can provide the required energy. Thus, charge storage based on the EDLC mechanism allows the system to be charged and discharged much faster than batteries. In addition, the equivalent series resistance of EDLCs is negligible, allowing them to work at close to 100% efficiency.
Unlike EDLCs, energy storage and delivery in batteries rely on chemical reactions. Redox-active materials are prone to decomposition; thus, the lifetime of the system will be continually diminished. In addition, the kinetics of the redox reactions limit the applicability of the device where high rates of charge and discharge are required. However, at slower rates of charge and discharge, the Faradaic charge storage mechanism provides much more energy for the system compared to that of EDLC based materials.
The energy and power of energy storage devices can be significantly boosted by combining positive and negative electrodes of different chemical natures into a hybrid configuration. In a hybrid energy storage system, battery-like materials, as energy sources, are combined with supercapacitor-like materials, as power sources. The energy density or specific energy demonstrates how long a device can be used and the power density or specific power represents how quickly a device can deliver energy. Integrating the two energy storage technologies into a single device can assure the best of both worlds and is considered as a practical key to unlocking the full benefits of energy storage. In addition, in a hybrid/asymmetric configuration, the positive and negative electrodes operate in different potential ranges, thus the operating potential window of the device can be significantly extended.125

2E and 3E cell setup
Generally, the electrochemical characteristics of electrode active materials are evaluated in either a 2E or a 3E system. A 2E cell setup is composed of a pair of closely spaced electrodes containing the electrode active materials and separated by an ion permeable, but electrically insulating, separator. Electrochemical performance of a 2E cell configuration depends on both the electrodes in a given electrolyte. Depending on the type of the electrode active material on each of the electrodes, supercapacitors are grouped into two major categories known as symmetric and asymmetric supercapacitors31 (Fig. 26). Electrodes in a symmetric device contain the same active materials with the same mass loading (m+ = m−) for both the positive and the negative electrodes. Symmetric supercapacitors are generally made of EDLC-based materials or materials consisting of contributions from both EDLC and Faradaic capacitances. Symmetric supercapacitors based on just Faradaic materials are rarely used since the redox reactions only take place either at the positive voltages or the negative voltages, but not at both.327 Asymmetric supercapacitors bring two different electrode active materials, i.e. one EDLC and one pseudocapacitive, together in the same electrolyte. Supercapacitors with the same type of electrode active material, but different masses of materials (m+ ≠ m−) on each electrode, are also considered as asymmetric supercapacitors (Fig. 26).
 Fig. 26  Schematic representation of the symmetric, asymmetric and hybrid configurations. (a) A symmetric supercapacitor that is constructed by using the same material for both the positive and negative electrodes. Symmetric supercapacitors commonly use carbon-based materials. Asymmetric supercapacitors cover a wide range of electrode combinations including (b) two EDLC electrodes with different materials, (c) electrodes of the same material, but with different mass loadings, (d) two pseudocapacitive electrodes with different compositions, (e) one EDLC electrode with a pseudocapacitive electrode, e.g. an activated carbon//MnO2 aqueous supercapacitor, (f) one EDLC electrode with a composite electrode (that can be a composite of carbon-based material with a metal-based species). (g) A hybrid configuration that is fabricated by using a capacitor-type negative electrode and a battery-like positive electrode, and (h) a hybrid architecture, typical of a Li ion capacitor, that is fabricated by using a battery-type negative electrode and an EDLC positive electrode. 
Optimization of the mass ratio between the positive and negative electrodes is even important in EDLC-based devices having the same electrode active material328 (Fig. 26c). Since the sizes of the hydrated anions and cations in the electrolyte differ, the surface area that is accessible for the anions or cations differs. The smaller the hydrated ions are, the better the kinetics is. Therefore, equal mass loading of the electrode active materials may prevent the EDLC-based supercapacitor from having the highest possible supercapacitive performance.328 In addition, considering the CV curve of the activated carbon electrode, it can be clearly seen that the voltammogram is liver-shaped, i.e. wide at one end and narrow at the other (Fig. 27). Ideally, the potential of zero voltage (PZV) of a symmetric supercapacitor should be in the middle of the capacitive potential range of the device, although this is not always the case.329 Supposing this, it can be clearly seen that the hatched area, corresponding to the discharge step, of the negative electrode is significantly larger than that of the positive electrode, which comes as a cost of putting a strong restriction on supercapacitive performance of the device. Therefore, to counterbalance the specific capacitance and energy of the device, mass loading of the negative and positive electrodes should be balanced.
 Fig. 27  The impact of mass balancing. CV of the activated carbon in a 3E cell setup. The contributions from each of the positive and negative electrodes to the overall voltammetric signature of the device are indicated with + and − signs, respectively, and the hatched areas represent the discharge step in each electrode. PZV stands for the potential of zero voltage in which the potentials of the negative and positive electrodes are equal and the voltage of the device is zero, i.e. the supercapacitor is fully discharged. CV in a 3E cell configuration is adapted with permission from ref. 330, copyright 2014, Elsevier. 
It has also been reported that appropriate unequalization of the capacitance of the positive and negative electrodes could also widen the operating voltage window of a supercapacitor that is fabricated by using the same material for both the positive and negative electrodes.331,332 This is because PZV is not necessarily in the middle of the capacitive potential range. Thus, the potentials at which the electrode active materials start to discharge on the positive and/or negative electrodes are not always symmetrical with reference to the PZV (see e.g.Fig. 28). In most cases a positive electrode has usually a narrower operating potential window than a negative electrode. Thus, at equal capacitance, the positive electrode will reach its maximum potential before the negative electrode attains its potential limit. Consequently, the positive electrode limits the operating voltage window of a symmetrical device, and causes the operating voltage to be smaller than the sum of the potential windows provided by each of the positive and negative electrodes. For extending the operating voltage window of the device it is thus important to properly measure the open circuit potential of each electrode. Then, the capacitance of the negative and positive electrodes must be calculated in the potential windows ranging from the open circuit potential to the limiting potentials of the electrodes. With proper implementation of this strategy the electrodes are forced to work across their entire operating potential range.332
 Fig. 28  CVs of the Cabot Monarch 1300 pigment black-poly tetrafluoroethylene (CMPB-PTFE) film electrode in a tube cell setup. Mass loading: 1.0 mg, electrolyte: 0.3 M K2SO4. The tube cell comprises two CMPB-PTFE electrodes and an Ag/AgCl reference electrode. The electrodes were connected to two potentiostat units to concurrently track the potential of either the positive or negative electrode against the Ag/AgCl reference electrode, as well as the voltage between the positive and negative electrodes. (a) Equal and (b) unequal (C+:C− ratio of 4:3) electrode capacitances that result in 1.60 V and 1.9 V operating potential ranges, respectively. Reproduced with permission from ref. 331, copyright 2012, Elsevier. 
Note that energy storage systems that contain one supercapacitive and one battery-type material in negative and positive electrodes are named here hybrid energy storage systems, not asymmetric supercapacitors. In general, asymmetric supercapacitors combine the advantages of the two different electrode materials that can operate at different potential windows, thus, achieving a high energy and a high power because of an extended operating voltage window (up to about 2.0 V in aqueous electrolytes, 2.7 V in organic solvents, and even up to 4.0 V in ionic liquids). It is worth mentioning that the charges stored on each of the positive and negative electrodes should be balanced between the two electrodes according to q+ = q−. (84)where m is the mass of the active material (g), ΔV is the potential range for the charge/discharge process (V), and CS is the specific capacitance (F g−1).
Optimization of the mass ratio between the negative and positive electrodes is of paramount importance. Let us look at the capacitance of a supercapacitor to clarify and better realize the importance of mass balancing in energy storage performance of the devices. For a two-electrode system, total capacitance, Ctot, can be obtained using: (85)The weight of active materials in each individual electrode is assumed to be m, while m− and m+ are the mass of the negative and positive electrodes, respectively. Thus, the specific capacitance of a single electrode can be calculated as: (86)and that of a cell is given by: (87)From eqn (85) and (87) the specific capacitance of a cell is given by: (88)Rearranging the above-mentioned equation, the specific capacitance of a supercapacitor is given by: (89)In a symmetric device, C+ = C− and m+ = m−, thus CS of the cell is 25% of either electrode's capacitance. In an asymmetric cell setup, C+ ≠ C−, and in most cases the specific capacitance of the pseudocapacitive materials (typically as a positive pole) can be regarded as extremely large compared to the specific capacitance of the EDLCs (typically as a negative pole), i.e. C− ≪ C+. In such a case, CS cell is given by: (90)The above-mentioned equation demonstrates that the specific capacitance of an asymmetric device is dominated by the electrode that exhibits a lower amount of specific capacitance. Thus, unbalancing the specific capacitance between the positive and negative electrodes of a supercapacitor severely limits its specific energy and overall supercapacitive performance.
As indicated previously, the operating voltage window of a carbon–carbon supercapacitor can be increased from 1.6 V to 1.9 V by increasing the C+:C− ratio from 1:1 to 4:3. For a cell with equal electrode capacitances, the cell capacitance, Ctot, as a function of the specific capacitance of the active material, CS±, is given by: (91)
Considering the point that for a cell with equal electrode capacitances V = 1.6 V, the specific energy, ES, can be expressed as: (92)The same expression for a cell with unequal electrode capacitances (in which m+/m− = 4/3 and V = 1.9 V) can be expressed as: (93) (94)It can be seen from the above expressions that the capacitance of the cell with unequal electrode capacitances is decreased slightly by 2.0% (i.e. 1/4 divided by 12/49), but the specific energy is increased significantly by 38.1% (i.e. 0.442/0.320), in comparison with the cell with equal electrode capacitances.331
We also simulated variation of CS,tot over the different m+/m− ratios ranging from 0.01 to 10 as well as the different C+/C− ratios from 1 to 50 (Fig. 29). The lowest and highest values of all the curves fall nearly at the same point. However, the distribution is not symmetric and the maxima of the distributions do not, therefore, correspond to the mean. As C+/C− becomes large, the distribution becomes more and more skewed to the right. From Fig. 29 it can also be obtained that by a 50-fold increase in capacitance of one electrode (here the positive one), the capacitance of the device just increases about 3 fold. Thus, concurrent improvement in capacitance of both the positive and negative electrodes is necessary to attain a high capacitance at the device level. Until now, while significant progress has been made in the fabrication of high energy positive electrode active materials, negative electrode active materials with high energy are much less explored. To enhance performance at the device level, the development of advanced negative electrode active materials is a must.
 Fig. 29  Variation of the total capacitance, eqn (89), as a function of the m+/m− ratio. The ratio of m+/m− changes from 0.01 to 10. The value of C− is fixed at 300 F g−1 as the typical capacitance of a carbon-based negative electrode active material, and the ratio of C+/C− changes from 1 to 50 by changing the value of C+ from 300 to 15000 F g−1. 
To optimize the energy storage performance of a hybrid system, masses of the e.g. battery-type positive electrode and supercapacitor-type negative electrode should be optimized as follows: (95)Without effective balancing in all kinds of the electrochemical energy storage devices, the electrode with the smaller capacity can be easily overcharged/over-discharged while the electrode with the larger capacity is only partially discharged/charged. It should also be noted that when synthesizing an electrode active material, it is not always clear if the material is in the charged, partially charged, or discharged state. Thus, to utilize the full charge storage capabilities of the materials in a cell, one should charge the electrodes individually, then, assemble the two fully charged electrodes in a cell/device and then discharge them. In this way the next time that the cell is charged, it should work properly and its charge storage capability will be fully utilized.
A 2E cell setup does not provide any information about the components of the cell and only considers the device as a whole. Thus, although the ultimate purpose of researchers is to fabricate a supercapacitor device (i.e. a 2E system), for an in-depth study of the active materials, and for obtaining detailed information on the individual components of a device, there is no other choice than to use a 3E cell configuration (this is also sometimes known as single electrode, 1E, measurement). The importance of a device depends on its performance. In designing a cell, all the components must be precisely designed and appropriately optimized to attain the best possible performance out of the materials. Thus, the importance of a 3E cell setup is that it provides us with plenty of information about each component of the cell.

Fig. 30 summarizes typical CVs and GCD profiles of a rechargeable battery, a hybrid device and a supercapacitor in both 2E and 3E cell setups. As can be seen, during the charging step (green traces) the potential of the positive electrode goes positive and the potential of the negative electrode goes negative, thus the potential window gets wider. During the discharge step (red trace) this process is reversed, and the potential of the negative electrode goes positive and the potential of the positive electrode goes negative. The potential separation between the negative and positive electrodes needs to be as high as possible to increase the energy content of the system. Potential differences between the battery electrodes in their fully charged and fully discharged states are indicated in Fig. 30. As denoted in the figure, a battery cell shows a potential difference even at a fully discharged state that equals the potential difference between the two near endpoints of the CV peaks. This is why batteries cannot be discharged to zero potential. In the case of a hybrid system, after full discharge, the minimum voltage of the cell can be zero, but doesn’t have to be zero.333 However, when a supercapacitor is fully discharged, the cell voltage is zero, and the potentials of the positive and the negative electrodes are equal.
 Fig. 30  Schematic illustrations of the CVs of the positive and negative electrodes in a 3E cell setup (left), CV of the device, i.e. 2E cell setup (middle) and GCD profiles in both 2E and 3E cell setups (right) of rechargeable batteries (top), hybrid systems (middle), and supercapacitors (bottom). The arrows indicate the direction of the potential sweeps and the green and red traces represent the charge and discharge processes, respectively. The potential difference between a fully charged and discharged battery cell is also represented in CV. Vavg.dis. denotes the average discharge voltage of a battery. Vmin can decrease to zero in hybrid systems (dotted line in CVs) and is typically zero in supercapacitors, but not in batteries because of the possible irreversible changes to the stable operating potential windows of the positive and negative electrodes. The light gray and golden areas under the CV curves or GCD profiles correspond to charge and discharge processes, respectively. 
As explained earlier, when the time of charging and discharging is the same, the coulombic efficiency of the device is 100%. However, the energy efficiency of the system is not necessarily 100%. This is especially the case in systems that show considerable pseudocapacitive behavior. Coulombic efficiency just looks at the x-axis of a GCD profile, whereas the energy efficiency uses the information that is obtained from both the x- and y-axes. Consequently, the area under the V–q plot that integrates the information of both the axes provides a more precise criterion for the real characterization of a system.
Note also that the quality of the delivered energy is not a state function (that depends only on the starting and ending conditions), instead it is a path function (that depends on the way from the initial to the final value). Thus, although the net values of energy and power of a system are extremely important, the way that the device delivers the energy is also crucial. In other words, two devices may have the same energy, but different performances for specific applications, since the ways of delivering the energy in the two devices are different. This is especially the case for Faradaic supercapacitors and hybrid devices in which the charge/discharge curves deviate considerably from linearity. Consequently, depending on whether a voltage plateau appears at a higher or a lower voltage, performance and real applicability of the system will be different. For example, the performance of the two devices with the same energy content could be different in lighting up a set of blue, green, yellow and red light-emitting diodes (LEDs) each of which is driven within a specific potential range. The device with a high voltage plateau can light up a blue LED for a prolonged time, whereas the one with a low voltage plateau could light up a blue LED for just a moment.
Cell/device notation
There is a large body of literature reporting on electrode active materials for both positive and negative electrodes, and therefore, there are many possible cell configurations. It becomes very cumbersome to indicate the positive and negative electrode active materials each time you would like to discuss a cell or device. As such, a shorthand notation, which electrochemists commonly use to describe electrochemical cells, should be followed. Operation of an energy storage device requires at least a negative electrode, a positive electrode and a separator that serves as a salt-bridge. To implement shorthand notation we move from left to right, starting from the solid material of the negative electrode on the far left, to the solution-phase components of the negative electrode half-cell, then we denote the solution-phase components of the positive electrode half-cell, and finally we indicate the solid materials of the positive electrode on the far right. In this notation, the phases are separated by a single vertical line “|” and the salt bridge or separator is shown by double vertical lines “‖”. Thus, an electrochemical cell may be represented by a negative electrode | a negative electrolyte ‖ a positive electrolyte | a positive electrode. For example, the overall reaction of the Daniell cell, which consists of Zn and Cu, is: Zn(s) + Cu2+(aq) ↔ Zn2+(aq) + Cu(s)(96)for which the cell notation is: Zn(s)|Zn2+(aq)‖Cu2+(aq)|Cu(s)
For simplicity, researchers in the energy storage community can content themselves with the illustration of just the electrode active materials, i.e. the negative electrode ‖ the positive electrode such as Zn‖Cu, or even more simply Zn//Cu, which seems to be known to the general public for this field of research. Reading this simple and most generic symbolic cell notation we know that the left side of the cell notation, i.e. Zn, implies the negative pole and the right side of the cell notation, i.e. Cu denotes the positive pole of the cell. It should be noted that, by convention, the left-hand electrode in this cell notation is always taken to be the anode, and the electrode written on the right side is therefore always the cathode. Having this point in mind, the cell notation is a shorthand description of galvanic cells, equivalent to the discharge process of any energy storage device.
Measurement of the key parameters
Electrochemical performances of energy storage devices are commonly characterized by cyclic voltammetry, galvanostatic charge/discharge, and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy. These methods are essentially used to measure the three fundamental parameters: current, voltage, and time. All the other parameters including energy, power, capacitance/capacity, equivalent series resistance (ESR), and the operating potential window can be obtained from them. Aside from the above-mentioned parameters, there are some other tests, namely, retention capability, cycling stability, coulombic efficiency, self-discharge, and time constant that provide insights into the performance of an energy storage system. There are still some other parameters that have not yet been well formulated, such as shelf life and temperature effects. Thus, it is essential to establish an improved process for their assessment, to support continuous improvement, and to unify the evaluation tests. However, these two parameters are better suited for field-testing and industry rather than laboratory experiments, thus they are not considered here. Additionally, testing a supercapacitor material (i.e. a 3-electrode cell setup) differs from a supercapacitor device (i.e. a 2E cell configuration), and the differences are clearly indicated here. It should also be noted that although almost all of the three main electrochemical techniques can be used for the measurement of each of the key parameters, each of these techniques has a particular advantage in determining some of these parameters. For example, EIS is better suited for the measurement of ESR, GCD profiles for the determination of capacitance, energy, and power, and CV for the differentiation between capacitor-type and battery-type characteristics. Before going deeper into the measurements of the parameters that are mainly used to show the performance of energy storage devices, we first define exactly what each of these terms means.
The operating potential window/cell voltage
The operating potential window is the maximum potential range that guarantees (i) chemical stability of the electrolyte against anodic and cathodic reactions, and (ii) stability of the electrode active materials against decomposition. A potential window is typically used for electrodes and a voltage window is commonly used for devices.329 The concept of potential/voltage window for batteries and supercapacitors differs slightly. In batteries, the potential/voltage window is defined as the potential range in which the changes of the electrode/device during the charging and discharging processes are reversible, whereas in supercapacitors in addition to being reversible during the charging and discharging processes, the behavior of the electrode/cell must be capacitive (with a rectangular CV or triangular GCD profile).329 Unlike batteries that display a discharge voltage plateau until the battery is nearly completely exhausted, the voltage of a supercapacitor is proportional to the amount of stored electrical charge. The maximum voltage of a supercapacitor is provided when the supercapacitor is in its fully charged state. The decomposition of solvent and irreversible reactions on one of the electrodes (i.e. over-oxidation or over-reduction of the electrode active materials) are among the parameters that restrict the operating potential window of an energy storage system. Let's denote the lower and upper potential limits of the negative electrode as EN1 and EN2, and that of the positive electrode as EP1 and EP2, respectively. The potential windows of the negative and positive electrodes are thus, EN2–EN1 and EP2–EP1, respectively.329
In many cases, especially in supercapacitors, the operating potential window of the positive electrode overlaps with that of the negative electrode, i.e. EN2 > EP1, which means the operating voltage window of the supercapacitor is narrower than the sum of the operating potential windows of the two electrodes. In an extreme case where the operating potential window of one electrode completely covers that of the other electrode, the operating voltage window of the supercapacitor is then restricted by the electrode with the narrower operating potential window. Referring to Fig. 31, this happens when EN1 < EP1 and EN2 > EP2 for a wider operating potential window of the negative electrode or EP1 < EN1 and EP2 > EN2 in the case of a wider operating potential window for the positive electrode. On the other hand, if the operating potential windows of the two electrodes are not connected, i.e. EN2 < EP1, which is commonly the case in batteries, the device would then have a minimum discharging voltage.
 Fig. 31  Operating potential/voltage window (OVW). Schematic illustration of the operating potential windows of the negative and positive electrodes as well as an asymmetric device, along with the potential of zero voltage (PZV). Reproduced with permission from ref. 329 copyright 2015, Springer Nature, with slight modifications. 
In a fully discharged state, the voltage across the supercapacitor terminals is zero and the potentials of the positive and negative electrodes are equal. The potential of zero voltage (PZV) affects the operating voltage window of the supercapacitor. If the difference between PZV and EN2 is greater than the difference between PZV and EP1 (PZV–EN2 > EP1–PZV), the positive electrode will be the voltage-determining electrode, and vice versa. The operating voltage window (OVW) of the supercapacitor device, with equal electrode capacitances, is then:OVW = 2 × (EP2–PZV); if PZV–EN2 > EP1–PZVOVW = 2 × (PZV–EN2); if EP1–PZV > PZV–EN2However, the operating voltage window of a supercapacitor with unequal electrode capacitances will be EP2–EN1. It has also been suggested that to widen the operating voltage window of a supercapacitor, the capacitance of the voltage-determining electrode should be further increased relative to that of the other electrode.332 Because the rate of the potential change on each of the positive and negative electrodes is largely determined by the capacitance of the electrodes, the higher the capacitance, the lower is the rate of the potential change.
Capacity/capacitance
Capacity is the amount of electric charge that a battery can deliver at the rated voltage (for more details on C-rate please see the section on Batteries). The total capacitance of a supercapacitor is given by: (97)Normalized capacitance, CN, is defined as the capacitance per unit mass, volume, or area of either the active materials or the whole package, eqn (98): (98)where x can be the mass, volume or surface area of the active material or even the whole device. The resulting normalized capacitance based on each of the aforementioned parameters is often defined as the gravimetric (or specific) capacitance (F g−1), the volumetric capacitance (F cm−3), or the areal capacitance (F cm−2), respectively.

Theoretical assessment. 
The theoretical capacity of a material can be easily calculated from Faraday's first law of electrochemistry that states one equivalent-weight (i.e. the atomic or molecular mass of a substance divided by the number of electrons involved in the redox reaction) of a material delivers 96485 Coulombs (or 26.8 A h), i.e.: (99)Thus, the amount of charge passed through an electrode is directly proportional to the amount of material deposited on or liberated from it. For example, the half-cell reactions during the discharge of a typical Li-ion battery are: Negative pole: LiC6 → C6 + Li+ + e−(100) Positive pole: CoO2 + Li+ + e− → LiCoO2(101) (102)Let us also refer to a lead–acid battery, which is characterized by the following half-cell reactions: Negative pole: PbO2 + H2SO4 + 2H+ + 2e− → PbSO4 + 2H2O(103) Positive pole: Pb + H2SO4 → PbSO4 + 2H+ + 2e−(104) (105)Thus, the theoretical capacity of a battery is defined as the number of electrons involved in the redox reaction multiplied by 26.8 A h. However, when comparing different batteries, the theoretical capacity per unit mass or volume of the material (CTheoretical(Bat.)) is of more interest than the absolute value of capacity. (106)where M and Vol are the molecular weight and volume of the electrode active materials, respectively. The values of capacity per unit mass, which is defined as specific capacity, for Li and Pb are: (107) (108)However, for most state-of-the-art applications, a battery has to occupy a limited space. Thus, it is even more important to know the capacity of a battery per unit volume of the material, i.e. A h cm−3. The capacity per unit volume of Li (densityLi = 0.534 g cm−3) is 2.06 A h cm−3, and that of Pb (densityPb = 11.34 g cm−3) is 2.93 A h cm−3.
In practice, we determine the practical specific capacity (CPractical(Bat.)) of the materials from the GCD profile by means of: (109)in which tcutoff is the time to reach the cut off potential (Vcutoff) in seconds, m is the mass of the materials, ∫Vdt is the area under the discharge profile, and V is the potential in the midway of the discharge step, i.e. at . The practical capacity is almost always less than the theoretical capacity, since the utilization ratio of the electrode active materials, which is defined as the mass ratio of the electrode active materials actually participating during the discharge process to the total mass of the active materials (ranging from 0 to 1.0), is very often less than 1.0. For example, in the case of Li-ion batteries, not all the Li ions can be removed from the lattice of the host material in the operating potential window and some of the Li ions are removed above the cutoff potential. In addition, to evaluate the practical specific capacity of a battery or cell, one has to take into account not only the integrated mass of the positive and negative electrodes, but also other essential components of a battery or cell, such as binders, conductive additives, separators, electrolyte, current collectors, and casing. Thus, in reality, the practical specific capacity of a battery or cell can be significantly different from the value calculated theoretically.
While the theoretical capacity of a battery can be easily calculated from the moles of available active materials, calculating the theoretical capacitance of a supercapacitive material is rather challenging. For EDLC-based materials, the capacitance is not only dependent on the type of the electrode active materials used, but also is very much dependent on the electrolyte used. For pseudocapacitive materials, there is not a fixed stoichiometry between the number of atoms and the number of electrons transferred in the redox reaction.1 For example, the oxidation products of RuO2, eqn (70), and MnO2, eqn (71), as well-established pseudocapacitive materials are most accurately referred to as RuO2−δ(OH)δ (0 ≤ δ ≤ 2) and MnIII(x+y)MnIV1−(x+y)OOMyHx (0 ≤ x + y ≤ 1). Thus, charge/discharge processes in metal oxides involve just a partial oxidation/reduction of the metal species. Moreover, the capacitance of a supercapacitor, either being EDLC or pseudocapacitive, is equal to the electric charge divided by the voltage. Thus, in contrast to the charge capacity, which is an absolute value, the capacitance is the charge capacity normalized to the voltage (potential), so the concept of theoretical specific capacitance without giving the voltage (potential) range does not make sense. Some pseudocapacitive materials, in a specified potential window, show a battery-like behavior with plateaus in their GCD profiles, for which one should not report capacitance, rather capacity. The limitations imposed by these factors on reporting the theoretical specific capacitance make such a criterion unreliable to be used for the assessment of the supercapacitive materials. However, to have an approximate assessment of the supercapacitive performance of the electrode active materials, the following approaches can be followed. The theoretical specific capacitance of an EDLC (CTheoretical(EDLC)) is measured as: (110)The theoretical specific capacitance of a typical activated carbon is about 354 F g−1 and the corresponding areal capacitance is about 18 μF cm−2 which are in good agreement with the experimental values of about 358 F g−1 (for a 2060 m2 g−1 carbon sample)334 and 15 μF cm−2.335 As can be seen, theoretically, the specific capacitance of an EDLC-based material is directly proportional to the specific surface area. However, in reality, it may not necessarily happen, especially for materials with very fine porosity and rich surface chemistry.336 For example, an activated carbon sample with a BET surface area of 1538 m2 g−1 gives a specific capacity of 321 F g−1, while another activated carbon sample with a BET surface area of 3038 m2 g−1 gives a specific capacity of just 207 F g−1.336
Theoretical specific pseudocapacitance (CTheoretical(PS)), which originates from Faradaic electron transfer processes across the electrode–electrolyte interface, is calculated as:337 (111)where n is the mean number of electrons transferred in the redox reaction, F is Faraday's constant (96485 C mol−1), M is the molecular weight of the pseudocapacitive material and V is the operating voltage window. For instance, in the case of the conducting polymer polyaniline (PANI), one electron is transferred per one aniline unit in the potential window from 0.0 to 0.8 V. –(An–An)n– + Cl− → –(An–An+·Cl−)n– + e−(112)where An represents the monomer of aniline in the polymer chain. The molecular weight of aniline repeat units in PANI is 91 g mol−1. Thus, (113)The theoretical specific capacitance of PANI is a combination of theoretical specific pseudocapacitance and EDLC of PANI. EDLC is typically on the order of a few tens to a few hundreds of F g−1. Thus, in an optimistic state and by generalizing the EDLC characteristics of the carbon-based materials to PANI, one can make an estimate of the theoretical capacitance of PANI. In practice, the total capacitance of an electrode active material is determined from GCD profiles and the contribution from EDLC and pseudocapacitance can be estimated using the step potential electrochemical spectroscopy technique, which is described later on.

Equivalent series resistance
Equivalent series resistance (ESR) is often defined as the sum of the resistances of the bulk electrolyte, contact resistance between the electrode and the current collector, as well as the resistance of the electrode.340 ESR is an important parameter that limits the speed at which a supercapacitor can be charged or discharged, thus a high ESR leads to a low power performance of the supercapacitor.341
Energy and power
Energy and power are the two most important parameters for characterization of electrochemical energy storage devices. Specific energy (W h kg−1) or energy density (W h L−1, or W h cm−2) is the amount of energy stored in an energy storage device per unit mass or volume/area of the electrode active materials or the whole device, respectively. Specific power (W kg−1) or power density (W L−1, or W cm−2) is the amount of power an energy storage device can deliver per unit mass or volume/area of the electrode active materials or the whole device, respectively. The relationship between these two important parameters can be expressed by the so-called Ragone plot, which is a very important plot for performance comparison of different energy storage devices.
Rate capability
Rate capability is defined as the capability of an energy storage device to preserve the capacitance/capacity as the current density or specific current increases.342 A system with fast kinetics of electrode processes (i.e. short diffusion pathway and fast electron/ion transport) exhibits a high rate capability and demonstrates a high power performance.343 Adequate control over the specific surface area and pore size distribution of the electrode active materials is crucial to attain a high rate capability. In a hierarchically structured porous material, the macropores shorten the ion diffusion distances to the interior surfaces, the mesopores provide low resistant pathways for the ions to diffuse through the porous particles, and the micropores are strongly desired to reinforce the electric double layer capacitance.344,345 As a result, three-dimensional hierarchical porous carbon demonstrates a better rate capability along with an enhanced electrochemistry performance compared with single-sized pore carbon-based materials or flat graphene sheets, because of the effectiveness of the processes occurring in a porous structure. Note that although a macroporous structure is beneficial for the high rate capability of the system, the large pore volume that is induced by the macropores reduces the packing density and the volumetric characteristics of the devices.345 Rate capability of batteries is assessed by the C-rate system, which is widely used to scale the charge and discharge current for batteries.123
Similar to the well-stablished C-rate system for batteries, a 60 s charge/discharge time has been recommended for supercapacitors.17,123 This 60 s charge/discharge time, referred to as a discharge rate of 60C, can remedy the inconsistencies that arose from the different test conditions in GCD experiments, i.e., A g−1, A cm−2, A cm−3, etc. In addition, it can be used to identify the proper scan rate in CV tests as ν = potential window/60 V s−1: ν = 1/60 V s−1 (∼16.7 mV s−1) for a 1 V system; ν = 2.3/60 V s−1 (∼38.3 mV s−1) for a 2.3 V one.123
Cycle life/stability test
Generally, the capacitance/capacity of an energy storage device decreases as it continues charging and discharging. There is not a generally accepted definition for cycle life of a device, and it strongly depends on the application. In some cases, cycle life is defined as the number of complete charge–discharge cycles before capacitance/capacity of the device falls below 80% of the beginning-of-life value.264,346,347 The death state of a battery for an electric or hybrid electric vehicle is also defined as 80% of its initial capacity.348 Note that in ordinary applications, we generally recharge the battery after using 70–80% of the full charge capacity, which increases the cycle life of the battery by several fold. Cycle life of batteries are typically limited to a few hundred to a few thousand cycles, due to the lack of stability of materials and reversibility of redox reactions during the consecutive discharge–recharge cycles, whereas supercapacitors can undergo millions of cycles, which makes them experimentally burdensome (if not impossible) to directly measure.123
As an equivalent to cycle life, cycle stability is a measure of how well an energy storage device deals with being consecutively charged and discharged. Cycle stability is commonly reported as a certain percentage of the initial capacitance/capacity that the device preserves after a specified number of cycles. In spite of the fact that redox reactions are involved in the pseudocapacitive charge storage, cycle life of pseudocapacitors far exceeds that of rechargeable batteries. This is because batteries store energy based on redox reactions in the bulk or through the intercalation of ions into the layered structure of active materials via bulk reactions of the electrodes. The intercalation process and the bulk redox reactions, however, are severely limited by the diffusion kinetics of the ions. Redox reaction-induced phase transformations may degrade the layered structure of the electrode active material with cycling, particularly at high rates.349,350 The non-ideal reversibility of redox reactions in electrode active materials is due to slow reaction kinetics, particularly if the reactions are driven at high rates, complicating the achievement of an ultra-long cycle life and a stable performance in batteries.349 In redox supercapacitors, electrochemical redox reactions take place at the surface or near the surface of the electrode active materials, but not through the bulk materials; thus, the negligible destruction of the electrode active materials structure ensures longevity of the redox supercapacitors.73 However, although research studies have shed some light on the main characteristics of the energy storage mechanisms of various systems, their molecular aspects remain mostly unknown. There is, therefore, an explicit need to conduct in situ experiments and simulations that can provide valuable mechanistic information, although conducting these experiments is a problematic task due to the difficulty in obtaining well-defined interfaces in redox supercapacitors.351
During a cycling test, most of the time is consumed at voltages that are not critical for device stability. Therefore, a more demanding test to evaluate the stability of a system is a constant voltage held at the maximum cell voltage known as the voltage-hold test.352 Typically, the voltage hold test takes a shorter time than the cycling test before a clear result can be obtained. In this test, the critical voltage is applied to the cell and by occasional (e.g. every 10 h during the voltage hold procedure) cycling the cell in a potential window that is harmless to the cell, capacity/capacitance of the cell is measured. Performing this test on energy storage devices, especially hybrid systems that utilize solution-phase redox species, gives more information on the long-term stability of the system because the voltage-hold test allocates more time for charged redox probes to diffuse away from the electrode surface and interact with other solution-phase redox species or the opposite electrode. Please see Fig. 32 to compare the fundamental difference between the two aging test procedures. In the case of cycling experiments and up to 12000 cycles, there are some capacitance fluctuations but no significant capacitance loss for both upper voltage limits of 3.5 V and 3.75 V (Fig. 32a). However, the results of the voltage-hold experiment for the test performed at 3.5 V and at 3.75 V are clearly different (Fig. 32b). While the voltage hold test at 3.5 V shows a stable performance with no significant capacitance loss, capacitance of the system for the voltage hold test at 3.75 V starts to decrease after just 200 h, demonstrating that the voltage hold test is better than respective cycling tests with identical upper voltage limits, and takes a shorter time than the cycling test.352
 Fig. 32  Cycling stability test for a supercapacitor utilizing a mesoporous carbon black and ionic liquid electrolyte [EMIM][BF4]. (a) Cycle test with upper voltage limits of 3.5 V (open squares, green) and 3.75 V (filled circles, red). Lower voltage limit during cycling is 0.0 V. (b) Constant voltage hold tests with hold voltages of 3.5 V (open squares, green) and 3.75 V (filled circles, red). Reproduced with permission from ref. 352, copyright 2013, Elsevier. 
Coulombic/energy/voltage efficiency
Coulombic efficiency (also called charge efficiency) is defined as the ratio of the charge drawn out from an energy storage device/electrode during the discharging step to the amount of charge that is stored in the device/electrode during the charging step. In a typical battery, coulombic efficiency is defined as the ratio of the discharge capacity to the charge capacity in one cycle.353,354 In Li-ion batteries, coulombic efficiency is also defined as the ratio between the Li ions that are stripped from the working electrode and the Li metal that is replated.355 For a system with 100% coulombic efficiency, charging and discharging times are the same in the GCD profile (if of course the rates of charging and discharging are the same).
Energy efficiency is a critical parameter to evaluate the performance of energy storage devices. While coulombic efficiency is deduced from the data along the x-axis of the i–V curve, energy efficiency utilizes the information of both the x- and the y-axes. Energy efficiency is defined as the ratio of electrical energy drawn out from a device to the electrical energy required to put the energy storage device back to the same state of charge. Voltage efficiency is defined as the ratio of discharge voltage to charge voltage of the energy storage system. Fig. 33 schematically shows the charge/discharge time and voltage of the different energy storage systems as well as their recovered and stored energy.
 Fig. 33  Schematic illustration of the coulombic, energy, and voltage efficiencies of the electrochemical energy storage systems. 
Self-discharge and leakage current
In an ideal energy storage device, the electrons just flow from the negative pole to the positive pole when the circuit is closed. However, in reality, under an open circuit condition, a slow discharge does occur which is known as self-discharge. This is because of an effective thermodynamic driving force that tends to move the charges back into the equilibrium condition. Often the self-discharge rate in supercapacitors is higher than that in batteries, thus this can have a severe adverse effect on the performance of a supercapacitor. From a practical point of view, it means that replacing, for instance, a vehicle's lead–acid battery with a supercapacitor is currently impractical since self-discharge can result in a supercapacitor having insufficient voltage to start the vehicle. The chemical and physical processes responsible for self-discharge are often complex. However, self-discharge processes are commonly classified under three main categories (Fig. 34):356,357 (i) charge redistribution, (ii) parasitic Faradaic reactions, and (iii) ohmic leakage of current between the electrodes. If one of the self-discharge mechanisms or a combination among them is available, self-discharge takes place.
 Fig. 34  Schematic illustration of self-discharge mechanisms: (a) charge redistribution is the main mechanism of self-discharge in EDLCs. Movement of ions between the Stern layer and the electrolyte and subsequently across the electrolyte due to movement of charge through the electrode active materials causes self-discharge in supercapacitors. (b) Self-discharge in redox pseudocapacitors, as well as in hybrid systems, is mainly caused by redox shuttling between charged redox species in solution (b, top magnified panel) or via cross-diffusion of charged redox couples between the oppositely charged electrodes (b, bottom magnified panel). Panel b is adapted with permission from ref. 317, copyright 2017, American Chemical Society. 

Charge redistribution. 
With many electrode active materials (e.g. porous carbon-based materials as well as pseudocapacitive materials), the outer portion of the electrode active material charges more rapidly than the interior of the materials. As a result, the surface-exposed portion of the electrode active material reaches the desired potential before the interior parts of the material. Inequality in the potential between the interior and exterior surfaces of the electrode active material is a driving force leading to movement of charge through the material to equalize charge propagation, called charge redistribution.356 In a supercapacitive energy storage system, the electrode potential is measured at the outer surface of the electrode active materials. Thus, as the charge moves deeper into the material, the measured voltage at the outer surface drops, i.e. the electrode self-discharges (Fig. 34a). Note that one of the characteristics of self-discharge by charge redistribution is that the self-discharge process will be attenuated by repeated charge/self-discharge cycles since with repeated charging more of the interior parts of the pseudocapacitive materials will be in higher oxidation states and/or more of the pores will be available for the electrolyte.


Parasitic Faradaic reactions. 
Self-discharge is also one of the critical challenges facing the realization of high-performance hybrid energy storage systems.317 In a hybrid system, solution-phase redox species diffuse across the cell and shuttle electrons between oppositely charged electrodes by exchanging electrons with either the electrode surfaces or other solution-phase redox species (Fig. 34b). Thus, one of the big advantages of a hybrid energy storage system, i.e. the storage of energy with the aid of solution-phase redox species, presents a major challenge for practical applications. Cross-diffusion of redox species also weakens the structure of the double layer. For example, on the positive electrode, the positive charge of the electrode is balanced by electrolyte anions in the double layer. The potential of the positive electrode may be higher than the thermodynamic oxidation potential of species on the electrode surfaces or at the electrode–electrolyte interface. The redox species that undergo oxidation reactions on the positive electrode pass their electrons across the double-layer into the electrode surface, thus, reducing the positive charge on the surface and releasing the anions from the double layer. In self-discharge due to Faradaic reactions, the processes are controlled by either an activation-controlled or a diffusion-controlled self-discharge mechanism. In a self-discharge process based solely on an activation-controlled Faradaic reaction, the reacting species are at high concentrations or are attached to the electrode, thus, transport limitations are removed. The self-discharge profile under this circumstance results in a linear voltage drop as a function of log time after a plateau.358 If the self-discharge profile is governed purely by impurities (e.g. O2, H2O2, Fe2+/Fe3+, etc.) at a nonporous electrode, the voltage would decay with t1/2, to satisfy the semi-infinite, diffusion-controlled Faradaic reaction.359


Ohmic leakage. 
In an energy storage device (full cell), there is the possibility of a short circuit between the positive and negative electrodes. The self-discharge due to charge transfer through this short circuit is called ohmic leakage. For a self-discharge process resulting from ohmic leakage, a plot of ln voltage versus time would give a linear relationship.359

Table 5 provides an overview of the self-discharge mechanisms. Of course, real energy storage systems are often complex and can have several governing self-discharge mechanisms, and the shape of the self-discharge profile is governed by the relative degree of each self-discharge mechanism. For more detailed mechanistic evaluation of self-discharge processes, readers are referred to some nice articles.356,358–363 To counteract the self-discharge current, which is the sum of the currents of all of the processes related to self-discharge, supercapacitors require the application of a small constant current, the so-called leakage current, to keep the device fully charged.
