In the next step, we utilize CV, GCD and EIS techniques for extraction of the key parameters of energy storage systems. However, self-discharge and leakage current can be simply measured using a voltmeter. Self-discharge processes can be examined in either a 2-electrode or 3-electrode cell setup. The most common method to assess the self-discharge of a cell is to leave a charged cell under the open-circuit condition and measure the voltage decay over time. Measurement of self-discharge in a 2-electrode cell configuration provides a realistic picture of the self-discharge as it may occur in a commercial device. However, mechanistic studies of the self-discharge processes in detail and the kinetics of each electrode cannot be studied in a 2-electrode system. To differentiate the self-discharge process of each electrode and identify its mechanism, a 3-electrode cell setup should be used. However, measurements of self-discharge in a 3-electrode cell configuration are blind to shuttle mechanisms, where the solution-phase redox species of one electrode diffuse to the other and undergo a redox reaction at that electrode. The evaluation of the leakage current and self-discharging processes by potential step methods is discussed next.
As a standard protocol for testing the leakage current, we recommend the procedure reported in Maxwell Technologies datasheets.364 For measuring the leakage current, an external resistor (Rext, e.g. 1 Ω) is connected in series with the energy storage device. The positive and negative terminals of the voltage power supply are connected to the positive and negative terminals of the cell, respectively, whereas the positive and negative terminals of the Rext are connected to the positive and negative terminals of the data acquisition unit, respectively. Meanwhile, a jumper is installed across Rext to expedite the charge cycle. For recording the leakage current, the cell is charged to the rated voltage by a charging current within the maximum current limit of the device, e.g. 1 mA. The voltage is held for 1 h and the jumper is removed. Then, the voltage is held for 72 h and the voltage across the Rext is recorded. Leakage current is given by: (118)The typical test setup proposed by Maxwell Technologies for the measurement of the self-discharge is to charge the energy storage device to the rated voltage and measure the voltage over 72 hours of open circuit at room temperature.364 For measuring the self-discharge, the cell is first charged to the rated voltage by a charging current within the maximum current limit of the device, e.g. 1 mA. The voltage is held for 1 h. Then, the voltage is recorded over 72 h while the cell is open circuited. Self-discharge can be expressed as: (119)

Cyclic voltammetry
Cyclic voltammetry is one of the most commonly used electrochemical methods and is based on the application of a triangular potential-time waveform to the electrode while monitoring the current that develops in an electrochemical cell. In a 2E cell setup, the voltage is applied between the positive and negative electrodes, whereas in a 3E system the potential is applied between the working and reference electrodes. The rate of change of voltage/potential with time is referred to as the scan rate. In CV, the current that flows through an electrochemical cell is plotted versus the voltage or time. CV is a very powerful technique and one of the most commonly utilized methods to evaluate energy storage performance of the electrodes (in a three-electrode cell setup) and devices (a two-electrode cell setup). The reversibility of the electrode reactions and mechanistic studies of the processes that are involved in energy storage systems as well as many other complicated characteristics of the systems can be readily studied by CV.123
Redox reactions of different species can be affected quite significantly by the adsorption of these species on the electrode surfaces. For the treatment of such a problem, one must choose an appropriate adsorption isotherm to study the system. Assuming only the oxidized form of a species is present initially, and that the number of species adsorbed onto an electrode surface is independent of potential, then:288 (120)where ΓOx(t) and ΓRed(t) are the amounts (mol cm−2) of oxidized and reduced forms of the species adsorbed at time t, respectively. This equation implies that reduction of adsorbed Ox produces adsorbed Red, and the sum of the two adsorbates is constant and equals Γtotal, i.e. equals the initial surface concentration of Ox, ΓOx(t = 0). Assuming a Langmuir adsorption isotherm: (121)in which bOx and bRed are the relative strength of adsorption of Ox and Red. For a Nernstian reaction: (122)From eqn (121) and (122), (123)Assuming adsorption of the Ox and Red species is essentially of the same strength, bOX = bRed, the equation can be rearranged as: (124)where θ is the fractional surface coverage. This equation is a unique form of the Nernst equation.
A GCD profile of the adsorbed species, or a thin layer of electrode active materials coated on an electrode, follows eqn (124). The area under the CV curve represents the charge required for the full reduction of the adsorbed species and is related to the surface concentration of the species as: (125)From the combination of the above-mentioned equations with: ΓOx(t) + ΓRed(t) = ΓOx,total(126)the expression for the current can be obtained as:288 (127)Assuming the number of electrons in the redox reaction n = 1, the geometric area of the electrode A = 1.0 cm2, the total surface concentration of the Ox species at t = 0, ΓOx,total = 2 × 10−9 mol cm−2, temperature T = 298 K (25 °C), the CVs calculated from eqn (127) at three different scan rates of 10, 30 and 50 mV s−1 are presented in Fig. 35a. For pseudocapacitive materials, the GCD profiles follow eqn (124) (Fig. 35b).
 Fig. 35  
CV and GCD profiles of a supercapacitor and an ideal capacitor. (a) CVs derived from eqn (127) at three different potential scan rates of 10, 30, and 50 mV s−1 with an assumption that the number of electrons in the redox reaction n = 1, geometric area of the electrode A = 1.0 cm2, total surface concentration of the species Γ = 2 × 10−9 mol cm−2, and temperature T = 298 K (25 °C). (b) GCD profiles derived from eqn (124) at the indicated constant currents of i, i/2, and i/3, considering the point that θ = it/nFΓOx,total. (c) CVs of an ideal capacitor of 50 mF at three different potential scan rates of 10, 20, and 30 mV s−1, derived from eqn (128). (d) GCD profiles of a capacitor at the indicated constant currents, derived from eqn (128). Panels b, c, and d are obtained with permission from ref. 1, copyright 2016, Taylor & Francis. 
Generally, the amount of charge that is stored on the plates of a capacitor is proportional to the voltage across the plates. This proportionality is referred to as the capacitance of the system, and thus can be generally expressed as: C = q/V. In CV, the applied voltage of the system varies linearly with time, i.e. V = V0 + νt. Thus, the capacitance of the system can be expressed as: (128)Eqn (128) indicates that the current flowing through a capacitor is linearly proportional to the scan rate and is independent of potential. Therefore, an I–V plot of the capacitor that stores charge in the electric double layer gives a rectangular shaped CV (Fig. 35c). It should be noted that ν is positive when charging the capacitor and is negative when discharging it. The GCD profile of an EDLC demonstrates a constant rate of voltage increase/decrease in charge/discharge steps, resulting in a triangular shape as demonstrated in Fig. 35d. CVs of EDLC and pseudocapacitors are quite different in shape (Fig. 35a and c), but they share two common characteristics;1 (1) the proportionality between i and ν, and (2) producing a current of reverse polarity with reversing ν.
In contrast to pseudocapacitance that occurs when reversible redox reactions occur at or near the surface of an electrode, batteries demonstrate less reversible CVs and kinetically slower processes. In batteries, the oxidation and reduction peak potentials of the voltammograms shift to more positive and negative values, respectively. The oxidation and reduction reactions are illustrated as plateaus on the GCD profile. Less reversibility of the CVs translates into an upward shift of the charge plateau and a downward shift of the discharge plateau in a GCD curve (Fig. 33c). Such a trend is basically responsible for the relatively low energy efficiency of a battery.
Resolving the capacitor-like and battery-like behavior
In CV, the current response to an applied potential varies depending on whether the redox reaction is a diffusion-controlled or an adsorption-controlled process. CVs of the active material in a 3E cell setup can be analyzed based on the shape of the curves. Active materials of batteries are characterized by distinct peaks in the CV. An almost rectangular CV curve is indicative of an ideal capacitive behavior. Pseudocapacitive materials are characterized either by rectangular CVs or CVs with highly reversible and significantly broadened peaks. Hybrid systems also display the characteristic CV curves of batteries or supercapacitors in a 3E cell setup. However, unlike the sharp redox peaks of batteries or rectangular CVs of supercapacitors in a 3E cell setup, hybrid energy storage systems, as well as some pseudocapacitors, display a relatively quasi-rectangular CV with a hump for the redox peaks, indicating a combination of battery-like and supercapacitive behaviors. As such, it is impossible to differentiate between battery-like and supercapacitive behaviors based solely on the shape of the voltammograms. Trasatti was the first to propose an approach to quantify contributions from diffusion-controlled and capacitive charge storage processes.365 He, and then others,236,366–369 examined the dependency of the voltammetric charge, q(ν), calculated by the integration of the internal area in the CV, on scan rate. The value of q(ν) can be deconvoluted into two terms: (1) qout that represents the contribution of the outer surface of the electrode active material to the charge storage (i.e. corresponds to the region directly accessible to the electrolyte), and (2) the inner voltammetric charge, qin, that comes from the partially accessible sites, such as pores, grain-boundaries, cracks, and so forth, for which the charge is proportionally related to ν1/2. (129)On the other hand (130)where qtotal is the total amount of charge (qout + qin).
The diffusion rate of the species into the bulk of the electroactive material could be controlled by the scan rate during a CV test. Utilizing eqn (129), as ν → ∞, more of the diffusion-controlled redox sites are excluded from being accessed by the species, since the Faradaic charge storage processes are too slow to occur at very high scan rates. Thus, extrapolation of the linear fit to q(ν) vs. ν−1/2 plot to the y-intercept (i.e. ν−1/2 = 0) gives the capacitive charge storage, qcapacitive. On the other hand, utilizing eqn (130), as ν → 0, the diffusion-controlled processes could access almost all available pores inside the bulk of the electroactive material. Thus, both Faradaic and capacitive charge storage processes occur concurrently and extrapolation of a 1/q(ν) vs. ν1/2 plot gives the total charge, qtotal. The difference between the capacitive charge storage and the total charge is assumed to equal the contribution from the Faradaic charge storage.
As a second analysis to develop a more quantitative interpretation of the data from a CV test and to separate the contributions from each of the aforementioned characteristics to the total charge, the dependency of the two charge storage mechanisms on scan rate should be explicitly considered. For a battery-type material, the current response varies with ν1/2, whereas for a supercapacitive process the current response varies directly with ν. Thus, as indicated before, the whole process can be described as: (131)To distinguish between contributions from each of these processes, the equation should be solved for the values of k1 and k2 at different potentials.

Eqn (131) can also be rearranged as: (132)By plotting i/ν1/2vs. ν1/2 the values of k1 and k2, as scan rate independent constants, are obtained from the slope and intercept, respectively.236,370–372 For example, Dunn and co-workers have studied the charge storage properties of MoO3−x (Fig. 36) and shown that capacitor-like charge storage in reduced R-MoO3−x occurs to a much greater extent compared with the fully oxidized F-MoO3 (75% versus 37%).372
 Fig. 36  Deconvolution of the capacitive and diffusion-controlled currents for reduced R-MoO3−x and fully oxidized F-MoO3 based on eqn (132). Capacitive charge storage for R-MoO3−x is 75% and that of F-MoO3 is 37%. Adapted with permission from ref. 372, copyright 2017, Springer Nature. 
Although this method has been widely used to deconvolute capacitive and diffusion-controlled charge storage processes, a plot such as this is sometimes non-linear in a particular potential window and usually only gives a straight line in a very narrow sweep rate range.373 It has also been argued that the data obtained in a particular potential range probably cannot be used for the determination of the charge storage mechanism(s) in another potential range.374,375 To deconvolute the total capacitive current into the contribution from Faradaic pseudocapacitance and double layer capacitance, step potential electrochemical spectroscopy has also been proposed; this is discussed in the chronoamperometry/chronocoulometry (potential step methods) section.
Operating potential window/cell voltage
The maximum operating potential window is determined by gradually increasing the potential window and recording the voltammograms to reach a potential close to the stability limits of the electrode active materials, or until the polarization trend of the solvent becomes very obvious. Moreover, the stability of the potential window is examined over extended times by the shape and intensity of the CVs. A decrease in intensity of the CVs may indicate that the electrode active materials are dissolving or losing contact with the substrate surfaces such as is seen in polymeric materials upon consecutive cycling. The change in shape of the voltammograms may indicate that the electrode active materials are being decomposed. In addition, gas formation could also block the pores of porous materials, thus shifting the shape of the CVs from a capacitive to a resistive curve. The type of electrolyte, configuration of the cell, and the electrode active materials significantly influence the operating voltage of a cell. An overwhelming advantage of non-aqueous electrolytes over their aqueous counterparts is their much wider stable operating potential window that spans experimentally up to 4 V in ionic liquids (and theoretically operate at up to 6 V).376 To avoid limited ionic conductivity, poor safety and toxicity of organic electrolytes, researchers have adopted another strategy that is moving from a symmetric to an asymmetric configuration. With a proper selection of the electrode active materials for both positive and negative electrodes and fabricating the device in an asymmetric configuration, one can make full use of the complementary potential windows of the electrode materials, thus achieving a maximum potential window in an aqueous cell system.329,377 Two- and three-electrode cell setups provide different information about the operating potential window. A three-electrode cell setup provides information about the electrode active materials (since the reference electrode provides a constant potential).378 To know what the redox behavior of each electrode looks like individually and how they contribute to the overall operating potential window of the cell, three-electrode studies of the electrode active materials are necessary. From a two-electrode cell setup, in which one electrode is assigned as a working electrode and the other electrode serves as both the counter electrode and the reference electrode, only general information about the operating potential window of the cell can be obtained (Fig. 37). However, from a practical point of view, a 2E cell setup is a straightforward way to obtain the practical operating voltage of the cell.
 Fig. 37  Potential/voltage window determination. (a) CV curves of activated carbon and Ni–Co–Fe layered double hydroxide (LDH) electrodes at a scan rate of 10 mV s−1 in a three-electrode cell setup. (b) CV curves of an AC//Ni–Co–Fe LDH asymmetric supercapacitor (i.e. two-electrode cell setup) at different potential windows from 0.5 to 1.5 V at a 1 scan rate of 20 mV s−1. Adapted with permission from ref. 275, copyright 2018, Elsevier. 
One piece of critical information emerging from the CVs in a 3E cell setup is that the area under the CV curve of the positive electrode active material multiplied by its weight should equal that of the negative electrode at the same scan rate in a well-balanced cell. This is a simple approach to assess whether the energy storage performance of the positive and negative electrodes counterbalance each other. To use this method of balancing, a slow scan rate should be selected, because a slow scan rate will not influence the electron transfer kinetics of the electrode active materials.
Capacitance
The capacitance of a cell can be obtained from the CV curve using: (133)where i is the current (A),  is the voltammetric charge obtained by integration of the area under the reverse peak of the CV with a dimension of “Amp. Volt”. ν is the potential scan rate (V s−1), ΔV is the capacitive potential interval (V), and x can be the volume, mass, surface area of either the electrode active material or the whole device. In eqn (133), and in an ideal situation, it should not matter either the area under the forward or the backward scan we choose. In such an ideal case, one can also use the integrated charge over the entire potential range divided by two. However, the efficiency is usually less than 100%, and non-idealities may make the capacitance obtained from the area under the forward scan higher than that obtained from the reverse scan. Non-ideality manifests itself in a number of different ways, e.g. rounding of the corners of the CV or the CV tilted from the horizontal (Fig. 38). Considering this point, and to account for non-idealities in typical supercapacitors, the calculation should be made using the data from the backward (discharge) scan.361
 Fig. 38  Schematic representation of the equivalent circuit models and the corresponding CV curves of (a) an ideal electrical double layer capacitor, (b) a capacitor in parallel with a Faradaic charge transfer process, and (c) a simplified supercapacitor model comprising an ESR, Faradaic resistance, and double layer capacitor. 
When using CV to study an energy storage device, there is always a trade-off between the energy and power, which can be determined from the potential sweep rate. The Faradaic current varies with ν1/2, whereas the capacitive current varies with ν, so the capacitive current becomes relatively more important at higher sweep rates. This is mainly due to the kinetic limitations of Faradaic reactions at high scan rates.379 Thus, in addition to the proper selection of the electrode active materials and device architecture,380 utilization of a suitable scan rate would essentially allow an optimal trade-off between energy and power densities of the energy storage device.381,382
Equivalent series resistance
The equivalent series resistance (ESR) of a system, which is composed of the electrolyte resistance, the inherent resistance of the electrode active material, and the contact resistance between the electrode active materials and the current collectors, slows down the rise in the current and rounds off the two corners of the CV at the beginning of the charge and the discharge processes. Therefore, the sharper the curve goes up, the less resistance the cell will have. Fig. 38 displays the CV curves of three different simplified equivalent circuits. An ideal EDLC exhibits a perfect rectangular CV curve (Fig. 38a). In reality, energy storage devices display much more complex behaviors; two simplified cases are presented in Fig. 38b and c. Fig. 38b displays a circuit comprising a Faradaic charge transfer process in parallel with double layer charging. The CV profile of such a system shows a sloping curve, the slope of which is equal to the reciprocal of the Faradaic resistance of the system (1/R). A simplified equivalent circuit of a pseudocapacitive system is also presented in Fig. 38c.
Since the current, i, is dq/dt, eqn (47) can be rearranged as: (134)where C is the capacitance (F), ν is the potential sweep rate (V s−1), and R is the resistance of the R–C circuit (equivalent to ESR in electrode/device). By fitting the initial part of the CV with the above-mentioned equation, one can obtain the ESR of the system.
As shown in Fig. 39a and c, the shape of a CV curve strongly depends on the scan rate. At low scan rates, the shape of a CV curve is close to rectangular, which resembles the characteristics of an EDLC. At high scan rates, the shape of a CV curve deviates seriously from its original rectangular shape and becomes tilted toward the vertical axis with rounded corners, thereby becoming a quasi-rectangle. Deviation of a CV from the rectangular shape can be ascribed to the overlapping effect of the double-layer and Faradaic (pseudocapacitive) charge storage mechanisms between the active materials and electrolyte and an increase in the internal resistance of the system.230,383
 Fig. 39  The effect of ESR (IR drop) on the shape of CV curves. (a) CV curves of carbonized bamboo fibers at different scan rates ranging from 1 mV s−1 to 300 mV s−1 in 3M KOH electrolyte. (b) Variation of specific capacitance as a function of scan rate in different electrolytes. (c) CV curves of a symmetric carbonized bamboo fiber-based device at different scan rates. (d) Variation of capacitance as a function of scan rate for a supercapacitor device. The steeper nature of the CV curve at the beginning of the CVs in panel (a) reflects their lower ESRs compared to the ones in panel (c). Adapted with permission from ref. 384, copyright 2016, Springer Nature. 
Energy and power
Energy and power of an electrode/device can be calculated from the capacitance of the electrode/device. However, the most widely used calculation method for the maximum specific power (W kg−1) or power density (W L−1) from the initial discharge process is:14 (135)where PN is the maximum normalized power, V is the potential (V), ESR is equivalent series resistance (Ω) and x can be the volume, mass, or surface area of either the electrode material or even the whole package.
Typical testing of an energy storage system does not provide direct information on the mechanism(s) that result in efficiency loss of the system. The plot of specific power and cell voltage as a function of current density, which is a common test in fuel cells and flow batteries,385–389 can provide plenty of useful information about the cell. Fig. 40 shows a plot of both specific power and cell voltage versus current density during a discharge process of a supercapacitor at time 10 s.390 As can be seen, specific power increases with increasing current density, reaching a maximum nearly halfway through the discharging of the cell voltage, and then decreases at higher current densities. The polarization curve (current–voltage curve) can be obtained in two ways: one is to fix the current density and monitor the changes in cell voltage, the other is to fix the cell voltage and record the current density.391 The power of the system can be obtained by multiplying current density by the cell voltage.
 Fig. 40  Specific power and cell voltage as a function of the cell's current density at the discharge moment at 10 s. Rct = 10000 Ω cm2, Cdl = 0.4 F cm−2, ESR = 0.05 Ω cm2, and the maximum value of voltage across the supercapacitor is 2.5 V. Reproduced with permission from ref. 390, copyright 2013, Taylor and Francis. 
Rate capability
In CV studies, rate capability is a measure of capacity/capacitance as a function of scan rate. Rate capability can be evaluated by plotting the variation of capacitance with increasing scan rate (Fig. 39b and d). As can be seen, the highest specific capacitance is obtained at the lowest scan rate, and the capacitance diminishes with increasing scan rate. A high rate capability demonstrates that most of the surface area and pores of the electrode active materials are accessible to the electrolyte ions even at high scan rates.392 Thus, the large specific surface and suitable porosity are extremely important to attain a high rate capability. However, in practice, the penetration distance of the electrolyte ions inside the electrode active materials diminishes with increasing scan rate, so that the inner portions of the active materials do not play a significant role in the charge storage process at high scan rates, thereby leading to a moderate or poor rate capability.390,393
Galvanostatic charge–discharge measurements
GCD is the most widely used method for analysis and prediction of the energy storage performance of the electrode active materials under practical operating conditions. In a GCD test, a constant current is applied to the electrode and the electrode is charged and discharged between two potential limits, while the change in voltage vs. time is recorded. Analogous to CV plots, supercapacitors, hybrid systems, and batteries demonstrate different responses in a GCD plot. The voltage output of EDLC and some pseudocapacitive materials increases and decreases linearly as current flows, while some other pseudocapacitive materials, as well as hybrid systems, demonstrate nonlinear charge and discharge curves with one or more tiny sloping voltage plateau regions. The GCD profiles of batteries exhibit a long steady voltage plateau derived from common thermodynamic principles. Because of this discrepancy among the GCD profiles of different energy storage devices, each type of material should be handled in a unique way to calculate its characteristic metrics.
Similar to CV for which the contributions from capacitive and Faradaic currents depend on scan rate, the current density of GCD testing affects the contributions from capacitive and Faradaic components to the total capacitance/capacity of the system. At high current densities, only the external surfaces of the active materials contribute to charge/discharge processes. Thus, the response of a hybrid device is dominated by the EDLC component, which results in a considerable decrease in its energy. However, at a sufficiently low current density, both the outer and inner sites of the active material(s) will be accessible to the electrolyte ions, leading to a higher value of the specific capacitance. A GCD test is also conveniently used to study the cycling stability of the device. Note that CV and GCD methods should both give the same result for analysis of an energy storage device, provided that ν for CV and i for GCD are given appropriate values. However, generally, CV analysis of an energy storage device is typically carried out in a shorter time interval than a GCD test. It should be noted that an extremely low current density should be avoided since this induces self-discharge.
The operating potential window/cell voltage
The cell voltage can be determined using the GCD test, by slowly increasing the voltage until a spike is observed. When there exists a plateau at a high cell voltage region of the charge profile, it implies that the current is consumed by the decomposition of the solvent. In such a case, the charging process should be immediately switched to the discharging step to preserve the stability of the system. Besides, it can be observed in a GCD profile that, with an increase in the voltage window beyond the stability limit of the electrolyte or electrode active materials, the coulombic efficiency of the system decreases (Fig. 41a).
 Fig. 41  (a) GCD profiles of a polypyrrole//self-doped polyaniline (PPy//SDPA) device at different potential windows from 0.75 to 1.5 V at a current density of 5 mA cm−2 in 1.0 M KCl. The inset shows the plot of specific capacitance vs. applied voltage that increases to 98 F g−1 at an applied voltage window of 1.3 V and then starts to decrease with further increases in the voltage window. The coulombic efficiency of the system is 95% at applied voltage windows of up to 1.3 and decreases to approximately 90% at a voltage window of 1.5 V. (b) GCD profile of the PPy//SDPA device, charged up to 1.3 V as well as the GCD profiles of the individual positive and negative electrodes charged to potentials of 0.7 V and 0.6 V vs. an Ag/AgCl reference electrode, respectively. Current density is 5 mA cm−2, and the electrolyte is 1.0 M KCl. Adapted with permission from ref. 256, copyright 2012, Elsevier. 
Thus, the optimal operating potential window is a trade-off between maximizing the energy storage performance of the system and minimizing the negative effect of the decomposition of the electrolyte and/or other materials. Furthermore, the behavior and contribution of individual positive and negative electrodes in the operating voltage window of a full cell can be monitored via GCD (Fig. 41b). For example, Fig. 41b shows that during a GCD test, a self-doped polyaniline positive electrode actually swings between 0 and 0.7 V vs. Ag/AgCl, while a polypyrrole negative electrode moves between 0 and −0.6 V vs. Ag/AgCl, and the full cell therefore operates in the voltage range of 1.3 V.
Capacitance
As we mentioned previously, non-idealities cause the CV curve to deviate from a perfectly rectangular shape; therefore, the integrated area in the forward direction may be larger than that of the backward sweep. Therefore, for accuracy, capacitance should be calculated based on the area under the reverse scan of the CV. The same reasoning holds in GCD experiments, thus, capacitance should be measured from the GCD test during a discharge step. More importantly, the calculation of capacitance using GCD profiles of each kind of system, including double-layer capacitive, pseudocapacitive, and hybrid systems, as well as being symmetric or asymmetric, requires selection of suitable mathematical expressions as standardized by Santamaría and co-workers.394
For an EDLC system, which exhibits a linear variation in voltage with time during the constant current discharge process, capacitance can be obtained using: (136)For a pseudocapacitive or hybrid system in which the GCD profile deviates from a perfect triangle, one should use eqn (137), which is a differential form of eqn (136). (137)However, whatever shape the discharge curve has, i.e. linear or nonlinear, the q/V value (which is not always equivalent to capacitance since the term capacitance cannot be used for hybrid systems) is calculated from the following general expression:78,394 (138)where q is the amount of charge, V is the voltage range (V), i is the discharge current (A), V is the potential (V), and x is a normalizing factor; i.e. mass (g), volume (cm3) or area (cm2) of the electrode active material or even the whole device. Note that if we use normalized current, ix, instead of i, the x must be removed from the denominator of the equation. The above-mentioned equation can be derived from the following principal equation: (139)If we multiply the right side of the equation by V/V and integrate the equation we obtain: (140)Note that in a GCD experiment, i is a constant, thus, not part of the integration. Rearranging the above-mentioned equation and dividing it by a normalizing factor, x, we can obtain eqn (138).
When the shape of the discharge profile displays the characteristics of a pure EDLC, i.e. a linear GCD profile, the above-mentioned equation is simplified as q/V = it/V = C in which t is the discharge duration(s). However, since an IR drop is unavoidable in a GCD test, for more accurate calculation of the capacitance in an EDLC system, the formula should adjust ΔV by excluding the ESR as follows: (141)It should also be noted that in some supercapacitive systems, internal resistance or equivalent distributed resistance (EDR) is represented as ESR plus an additional resistance that comes from the charge redistribution processes in the electrode pores. Contributions from the latter part to the total resistance manifest as a curvature at the beginning of the discharge profile, just after the ESR. Thus, for a practically useful linear approximation of eqn (138), one should also exclude the part with curvature from the discharge profile178 (Fig. 42a), and the equation can then be simplified as: (142)Note that eqn (138) holds in all cases; however, for a nonlinear GCD profile, q/V is not the capacitance. In other words, for a non-linear GCD profile, the capacitance concept should not be applied, although the q/V ratio can always be obtained with the unit of F. Instead, for a fair comparison of the energy storage capability of the systems with nonlinear GCD profiles, charge storage capacity and energy should be used.
 Fig. 42  (a) Discharge profile of a supercapacitor in which the equivalent series resistance (ESR) and the equivalent distributed resistance (EDR, that represents ESR and the resistance in the pores) are indicated. GCD profiles of the positive (b) and negative (c) electrodes in a 3-electrode cell setup. The direction and the area corresponding to the charge and discharge steps are indicated in the panels. 
One of the most common mistakes we are all likely to make is in calculating the capacitance, as well as the other parameters, from the GCD profiles of the negative electrode active materials in a 3-electrode cell setup. In such a case, the charging step is falling and the discharge step is rising. One should take care to consider the area under the discharge step for extraction of the parameters. The area under the discharge step is actually located on top of the curve as schematically presented in Fig. 42c.
Generally, the mass of the active materials or the thickness of the electrode in energy storage devices significantly influences the performance of the device. In some cases, especially for commercial applications, to optimize a device for a particular application, mass loading and thickness of the electrode active materials are tuned. For example, a supercapacitor with 10 μm thick electrodes may be optimal for high power applications whereas one with several hundred μm thick electrodes may be more suitable for high energy applications.395 Thus, for a supercapacitor with an extremely thin active layer or very minute amounts of active materials, the calculated specific capacitance, CS, is superior in quantity, but is often not practically usable.396 While the gravimetric performance of the electrodes/devices decreases with increasing mass loading, the areal performance increases almost linearly with mass loading. Therefore, to avoid over-estimation of a material's performance, it is necessary that mass loading, volume, area and thickness of the active material be reported to provide more reliable criteria for comparison of different devices.123
Energy and power
The energy contents of a supercapacitor during a charging/discharging process under a constant current can be generally described as:78 (143)For a linear GCD plot, Vt is a linear function of t, and eqn (143) is simplified as E = 1/2CV2. Boosting the voltage can significantly increase both power and energy performance of the system. Since energy storage is the primary role of an energy storage device, energy and power are key parameters to compare energy storage performance of all the devices. However, confusion arises when some researchers attempt to use the C = Δq/ΔV equation for nonlinear GCD profiles, derived from peak-shaped CVs, as the capacitance for the calculation of energy and power of the system. The energy (E) stored in the capacitor is the work done to charge up the capacitor with a charge q. When the potential is a linear function of time, eqn (143) is simplified as eqn (10). However, for a non-linear GCD profile eqn (10) cannot be applied, instead the integral of the area under the GCD profile should be used. On the other hand, CVs and GCDs are recorded for both 2E and 3E systems and the corresponding capacitances can be obtained. However, energy and power densities can only be realized in a 2E system, but not from a 3E cell setup. The exception is the symmetric supercapacitor for which the specific capacitance estimated from the three-electrode configuration is simply four times higher than that from the two-electrode system. Because, in contrast to a 3E cell setup that measures the performance of an individual electrode, a 2E system is interpreted as two pairs of capacitors connected in series. The total capacitance of the two capacitors connected in series is obtained from eqn (69). In the case of symmetrical supercapacitors, it is reasonable to assume that the capacitance of the positive electrode is equal to that of the negative electrode. With this assumption (Cp = Cn): (144) Celectrode = 2Ccell(145)where Celectrode is the capacitance of a single electrode. Dividing both sides of eqn (145) by the normalization factor, N, provides: (146)The normalizing factor of a symmetric supercapacitor is twice the value of the mass or volume of a single electrode, Ncell = 2Nelectrode, thus: (147)In this equation, Ccell/Ncell is the capacitance of the device normalized to the total weight or volume of the device, whereas Celectrode/Nelectrode is the capacitance of a single electrode normalized to the weight or volume of an individual electrode. Consequently, Celectrode,N = 4Ccell,N(148)eqn (148) can be used to extrapolate the capacitance of an electrode, measured in a 3E cell setup, to the performance of a symmetric device. Note that, for a supercapacitor device, the area-normalized capacitance (F cm−2) is only meaningful if the device is of the planar type. Areal capacitance is simply the ratio of the cell capacitance, Ccell, to the geometric area of the device, which is actually the same for the positive and negative electrodes and the separator. In this case, the above-mentioned equations can be simplified as presented in the far right side of each equation. Note that in the measurement program of some energy storage devices, the device is charged to its rated voltage at a specified current, rested at open-circuit voltage for a specific period of time, then discharged to a low safe voltage at its specified current. Rate capability
Rate capability is one of the most important characteristics for energy storage devices and is studied by measuring the capacitance/capacity of a device at different current densities (Fig. 43). A device with a high rate capability shows almost stable capacitance at each current density.399 EDLC materials often demonstrate high rate capabilities, whereas pseudocapacitive materials, which rely of Faradaic redox reactions for charge storage and typically suffer from high internal resistance (intrinsically low conductivity), often result in compromises of rate capability.400
 Fig. 43  (a and b) GCD profiles of a nanostructured CuCo2O4 electrode at different current densities from 1 to 50 A g−1. (c) Rate capability of the system at various current densities. (d) Cycling performance and coulombic efficiency of the system at different current densities. Reproduced with permission from ref. 235, copyright 2014, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Cycling stability
Cycling stability, which is a crucial factor for characterization of an energy storage device, can be readily measured by continuously repeating GCD cycles at a given current density for a few hundred (in batteries) to several thousand (in supercapacitors) cycles.401 The result is often reported as the percent of the initial capacitance/capacity that the device retains after a specified number of cycles. This test can also be performed by recording the GCD cycles at ascending current densities (for tens to thousands of cycles each) that finally return to the initial value (Fig. 43d). The cycle life of a rechargeable energy storage system depends heavily on the operating conditions such as the depth of discharge, current of charge and discharge, and operating temperature. Choosing a standard cycling lifetime value depends entirely on the nature of the energy storage system. Non-Li-ion batteries deliver 500 full charge/discharge cycles, Na- and Li-ion batteries can on average last ∼2000 cycles, for supercapacitors ∼10000 cycles have been proven feasible by the literature, and cycling lifetime of the hybrid system is in between (∼5000).316,402 We recommend this as a standard protocol for consistent assessment of energy storage systems.
Coulombic/energy/voltage efficiency
Coulombic efficiency, CE, is one of the criteria for the characterization of energy storage devices that quantifies the efficiency of electron transfer within an electrode/device. CE is calculated using the following equation:403,404 (157)where qd and qc are electronic charge extracted from and inserted into the electrode active materials, respectively, td and tc are discharging and charging times, respectively, and id and ic are discharging and charging currents, respectively (Fig. 43d). When charging and discharging a cell with the same current, the formula can be simplified as:405 (158)Coulombic efficiency is influenced by current density and temperature, so the values of these parameters should be specified when reporting cell characteristics.
Energy efficiency, EE, can be obtained via:403 (159)where Ed and Ec are discharging and charging energies, i.e. the areas under the discharge and charge profiles, respectively, and Vd and Vc are the discharge and charge voltages, respectively. A supercapacitor is able to be charged and discharged much faster than a battery with nearly 100% efficiency.406 Voltage efficiency, VE, can be simply obtained from the CE and EE by the following expression:407 (160)
Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy
EIS is a widely used and non-invasive technique for characterization of a variety of energy storage systems, especially supercapacitors (since batteries are inherently non-linear systems, i.e. they demonstrate a non-linear i–V relationship, and, on the other hand, linearization is inherent to the EIS technique, EIS studies of batteries may be prone to degeneracy).408 EIS testing measures the impedance of a system as a function of frequency at a fixed potential. EIS can be used to measure the capacitive behavior of either an individual electrode or a device comprising two or more electrodes.409 In an EIS measurement, a small amplitude sinusoidal voltage (normally from 5 to 10 mV) is superimposed on a steady-state voltage and the resulting impedance is recorded as a function of the modulation frequency. The read-out signal of an EIS study is conveniently represented as a Bode plot (magnitude and phase response vs. frequency) or a Nyquist plot (imaginary vs. real part of the impedance). Practically, EIS testing can be used to study the impedance, specific capacitance, equivalent series resistance, and charge storage mechanisms for electrode active materials.123 More interestingly, adjusting the frequency makes it possible to act on the different conduction paths in an equivalent circuit. It means that depending on the applied frequency the physio-chemical characteristics of the material can be tuned so that the material behaves like a pure resistor, capacitor, etc.
A rewarding approach to analyzing EIS data is fitting the experimental data to a suitable equivalent circuit model. However, scientists may know little about the processes or mechanisms that are involved in the system. As such, the relative ease with which a set of experimental impedance data can be fit to different equivalent circuits makes the quantitative EIS analysis a challenging task. Thus, scientists should take care so that when they do EIS analysis, the equivalent model and consequently the resulting data make sense. Before going deeper into EIS-based characterization of an EES system, it's very important to understand the principles on which the EIS technique was conceived and developed.
As is well known, the electrical resistance (R) is the ability of a circuit element to resist the flow of electrical current (I) when a voltage (V) is placed across two points of a conductor. When current flows through a circuit, the relationship between I and V can be described by Ohm's law: (161)
However, in the real world, circuit elements exhibit much more complex behavior. Thus, the simple concept of resistance cannot properly explain a system, and in its place, we use impedance (Z) as a more general circuit parameter. Although impedance is similar to resistance, it is a time- or frequency-dependent parameter and is defined as: (162)in which Vt and It are time-dependent voltage and current, respectively. Electrochemical impedance is usually measured by applying a sinusoidal potential excitation to an electrochemical cell and then measuring the current flow through the cell. The general formula for the sinusoidal voltage is given by: Vt = V0sin(ωt)(163)where Vt is the voltage at time t, V0 is the amplitude of the sinusoidal voltage wave, ω (rad s−1) is the angular frequency (ω = 2πf, f (Hz) is frequency, i.e. the number of cycles completed in one second), and t is the time. In a linear system, the current response, It, is shifted in phase (φ) and the equation for the resulting sinusoidal current wave is: It = I0sin(ωt + φ)(164)in which It is the current at time t, and I0 is the amplitude of the sinusoidal current wave. It should be noted that direct current or voltage can be considered as a special type of alternating current or voltage whose frequency is zero.
Based on eqn (162), the impedance of the system can be calculated as: (165)Complex algebra is commonly used in electrical engineering, when a quantity has a phase as well as a magnitude, to simplify analyses of linear circuits. Let the symbol Z represent a complex number while Zre (or Z′) and Zim (or Z′′) are its real and imaginary parts: (166)If the real part, Zre, is plotted on the x-axis and the imaginary part, Zim, is plotted on the y-axis, a “Nyquist plot” is obtained, in which the impedance is represented as a vector of length ∣Z∣ and the angle between the∣Z∣and the y-axis is the phase angle, φ (Fig. 44a). As represented in the complex plane, the length of the vector Z can be expressed as: (167)Looking at Fig. 44a, one can see that Zre = |Z|cosφ(168) Zim = |Z|sinφ(169)So, the complex number (eqn (166)) can be written as: Z = |Z|cosφ + j|Z|sinφ = |Z|(cosφ + jsinφ) = |Z|ejφ(170)where the last step makes use of Euler's formula: ejφ = cosφ + jsinφ(171)Generally, the EIS spectrum of a cell can be presented in either a Nyquist or a Bode plot (Fig. 44).
 Fig. 44  (a) The complex plane of the complex number Z. (b) An equivalent modified Randles’ circuit. Representation of the (c) Nyquist plot and (d) Bode plots for the modified Randles circuit simulated over the frequency range from 1 MHz to 1 mHz, ESR = 100 Ω, Rct = 200 Ω, a = 0.001 Ω−1 s0.8 and n = 0.8 (see eqn (195)), and Rw = 1000 Ω, X = 5000, and α = 0.5 (see eqn (193)). The parameters that can be extracted from the Nyquist and Bode plots are presented in the figures. The points marked with I–III in the Bode phase (circles) and magnitude (diamonds) plots are obtained from the I–III vectors presented in the Nyquist plot. φ is obtained from the angle that each vector makes with the x-axis and |Z| is the length of the vector. 
More popularly, EIS spectra are presented as a Nyquist plot, since a Nyquist plot provides more visible characterization information about the system under study.361 However, a Bode plot provides a clearer description of the frequency-dependent electrochemical behavior of the system than a Nyquist plot. In a Bode plot, the logarithm of impedance magnitude |Z| and the phase angle (φ) are plotted vs. the logarithm of the frequency (f).

Note that the Nyquist plot masks the high-frequency response where impedance is low, whereas the Bode plots, which are representations in the log frequency domain, provide a much better resolution for the high-frequency data. 
In a parallel R/C circuit, the overall impedance of the system is expressed as: (182)Then: (183)Transferring eqn (183) to the standard form of a complex number, we have: (184)Therefore, Zre, Zim, and φ of the parallel R/C circuit are given by: (185) (186) (187)Impedance spectra (Nyquist and Bode plots) for a circuit containing a resistor in parallel with a capacitor are presented in Fig. 46. As can be seen, the impedance of a parallel R/C circuit is a semicircular arc. At low frequencies, ωCR ≪ 1, Zre ≈ R, and Zim ≈ 0, thus the R/C circuit acts as a resistor. At sufficiently high frequencies, ωCR ≫ 1, Zre ≈ 0, and Zim ≈ 1/ωC, thus the R/C circuit acts as a capacitor. If ωCR = 1, Zim has a maximum value of R/2 that is the peak point of the semicircle.
 Fig. 46  Simulated Nyquist plot (a) and Bode plots (b) of a resistor in parallel with a capacitor over the frequency range from 1 MHz to 1 mHz, R = 100 Ω, and C = 0.01 F. The inset of panel (a) shows the equivalent circuit. 
In an ideal supercapacitor, the current passing through the cell is either the double layer charging/discharging current or the Faradaic current. However, several undesired processes including: ohmic leakage, unwanted Faradaic redox reactions, and charge redistribution356,357 can cause a leakage current and lead to charge transfer across the double layer. Thus, the equivalent circuit for a supercapacitor, comprising both EDLC and pseudocapacitance, involves: an equivalent series resistance (ESR), a double layer capacitance, Cdl, a Faradaic resistance, Rct, a pseudocapacitance, CPC, and a parallel resistance of the leakage reaction, RL (Fig. 47).
 Fig. 47  Three equivalent circuits that are commonly used for the description of supercapacitors (a–c). The circuit elements are as follows: ESR, the equivalent series resistance of the cell; Cdl, the double layer capacitance; Rct, the charge transfer resistance corresponding to the process of charging the pseudocapacitor through an electron transfer process; CPC, pseudocapacitance; and RL, the leakage resistance. 
Note that pseudocapacitance of the system is parallel to the double-layer capacitance. The total impedance of the circuit presented in Fig. 47a can be expressed as:390 (188)where a = 1 − ω2CdlCPCESRRctRL and b = ω(CdlRct + CPCRL + CPCRct). The simulated Nyquist plots for the equivalent circuit presented in Fig. 47a are presented in Fig. 48.
 Fig. 48  Simulation of the Nyquist plots with different values of Cdl and RL. Simulated Nyquist plots of the equivalent circuit presented in Fig. 47a showing the effect of (a) Cdl with three different values of 1, 0.3, and 0.01 F and (b) RL with three different values of 5, 50, and 5000 Ω on the shape of the resulting Nyquist plots. The magnitudes of the other parameters used to simulate these plots are shown on the plots. 
For the circuit presented in Fig. 47a, if the kinetics of the leakage reaction is very slow, RL → ∞ then the equivalent circuit is simplified as Fig. 47b. The equivalent circuit presented in Fig. 47b has previously been used to model a pseudocapacitive system.390 For such a circuit, two limiting cases arise:
At very low frequencies (ω → 0) or fast kinetics of the Faradaic reactions (Rct is low): (189)At sufficiently high frequencies (ω → ∞) or slow kinetics of the Faradaic reactions where jωCdl ≫ 1/Rct: (190)If the kinetics of the Faradaic reactions responsible for pseudocapacitance of the system are very slow, Rct → ∞ and Cpc → 0 and the equivalent circuit is simplified as shown in Fig. 47c. It should be noted that the equivalent circuits presented in Fig. 47 are for a non-porous electrode active material. The classic equivalent circuit to model energy storage systems, especially supercapacitors with porous electrode active materials, could be a modified Randles equivalent circuit (Fig. 44). An ideal Randles circuit involves a double layer capacitor, Cdl, whereas a modified Randles circuit uses a constant phase element (CPE) in place of Cdl. A modified Randles circuit is composed of an equivalent series resistance (ESR) in series with a parallel circuit characterized by a branch with CPE and another branch composed of Faradaic impedance (i.e. a combination of a charge transfer resistance, Rct, and a Warburg impedance, W). The Warburg element is seen where the charge transfer process is also influenced by diffusion to and from the electrode–solution interface. Thus, in such a cell, polarization is controlled by a combination of both kinetic and diffusion processes. The impedance of the Warburg element can be calculated from: (191)in which: (192)where R is the ideal gas constant, T is the temperature, n is the number of electrons, F is Faraday's constant, DO and DR are the diffusion coefficients of the Ox and Red species, and CO* and CR* are the bulk concentrations of Ox and Red species, respectively. The impedance of the Warburg element corresponding to open circuit finite diffusion with a reflective boundary condition is given by:410 (193)and that for a short circuit terminus is given by: (194)where Rw is a resistance, X is equal to L2/D (L is the effective diffusion thickness, and D is the effective diffusion coefficient), and α is the Warburg exponent.
The impedance of the CPE is given by: ZCPE = a−1(jω)−n(195)where a is the frequency-independent constant relating to the surface properties, and the exponent n arises from the slope of log Z vs. logf (−1 ≤ n ≤ 1). The physical meanings of the coefficient a for n = −1, 0, 0.5, and 1 are listed in Table 6.

Total impedance of the modified Randles circuit is thus calculated by: (196)We have (197)then (198)If the rate of charge transfer is extremely faster than the rate of mass transfer, the Nyquist plot will consist of just a Warburg element as a diagonal line with a slope of 45°. If the electrochemical system is kinetically sluggish, the semicircular region will be significantly larger and the Warburg impedance may not be visible. Thus, the shape of the Nyquist plot for a Randles equivalent circuit is determined by a combination of the diffusion process and electrode reaction kinetics.
