Capacitance
EIS (both in the form of Nyquist plots and Bode plots) is a powerful technique for characterization of energy storage systems.412 The Randles circuit and its modified versions are the most common equivalent circuit models that describe the behavior of a supercapacitor quantitatively. Neglecting the Warburg impedance, the Nyquist plot can be simplified as a semicircle. The total impedance of a cell can be represented as: Z = Zs + Zdl(199) Zs = Rs(200) (201)Thus, (202)Let us consider two limiting cases for eqn (202): (1) at a low frequency, where ω → 0, and Z approaches the sum of the solution resistance and the charge transfer resistance (Z → Rs + Rct), and (2) at high frequency, where ω → ∞, and Z approaches the solution resistance (Z → Rs). In other words, the intercept of the Nyquist plot with Zre at the highest frequency is Rs (more precisely, the ESR), and the diameter of the semicircle is the charge transfer resistance, Rct. The maximum value of Z (top of the semicircle) occurs when ωRctCdl = 1, from which the capacitance can be simply measured using: (203)Simon and co-workers introduced another method for the calculation of C,413 that is:31,381 (204) (205) (206)where Z is the overall complex impedance, ω is the angular velocity given by 2πƒ, Zre is the real part of the total impedance, Zim is the imaginary part of the total impedance, and Cre and Cim are the real and imaginary capacitances, respectively.413 The specific capacitance on a one-electrode basis can be calculated as:414 (207)where m is the mass of the materials on the working electrode.
Equivalent series/distributed resistance
EIS is a typical method for the evaluation of the ESR and EDR of a system. An ideal capacitor is represented by a straight line in a Nyquist plot that is vertical to the real axis with the real part of Z being the ESR. However, a supercapacitor approaches a near vertical line at low frequencies shifted on the real axis by the total internal resistance. The equivalent circuit of a pseudocapacitive system at high frequencies is reduced to a simple R–C circuit. The second term on the right side of eqn (202) will be much smaller than the first term at the high end of the AC frequency, therefore the corresponding impedance will be the ESR.288 So, ESR is obtained from the intercept of the Nyquist plot with the real axis, Zre, at the highest frequency.
EDR is calculated as the projected value of the vertical portion of the Nyquist plot at low frequencies onto the real axis, Zre (after subtraction of the ESR value and the contact resistance of the active materials on the current collector), as schematically represented in Fig. 50, RBC.415–418 Kötz and co-workers have also experimentally obtained EDR as the difference between the intersection point of the 45° line in the Nyquist plot with the real axis and the point where extrapolation of the low frequency part and the 45° line of the Nyquist plot coincide.419 Unfortunately, although there has been much work on EIS characterization of electrochemical energy storage systems, there are multiple and sometimes contradictory interpretations of the Nyquist plots based on the ESR and EDR values.415,419–421 In a nice study, Pilon and co-workers have tried to provide an unequivocal interpretation of the numerically simulated Nyquist plots for both EDLC electrodes and devices, and experimentally confirmed the results and interpretations by fabricating an AC//AC device (Fig. 50).340 EDR can also be approximated from the fit data as: (208)where WR corresponds to the resistance for the open-circuit Warburg model and the series combination of diffusion resistance.419 The intersection between the low frequency vertical line and the Zre axis corresponds to internal resistance (Rint) or the overall resistance of the electrode or the device.422
 Fig. 50  Schematic representation of a Nyquist plot for EDLC electrodes/devices. RA stands for ESR, and RBC is ascribed to EDR. Note that there are multiple interpretations of the RA, RB, RAB, RC, and RBC values for the details of which the readers are referred to ref. 340 from which the scheme has been obtained. Adapted with permission from ref. 340, copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. The inset shows a schematic representation of the Nyquist plot of an ideal capacitor (line 1) and an electrochemical capacitor with porous electrodes (line 2). Adapted with permission from ref. 416, copyright 2000, Elsevier, with slight modifications. 
Note that the ESR of the cell is not significantly influenced by the electrode active materials’ parameters and is mainly determined by the various interfacial (contact) resistances in the cell, the electrolyte resistance, and the electronic resistance of the electrode active materials. In contrast to the ESR, the value of EDR is markedly influenced by the electrode active materials’ parameters (Fig. 51).415 Densification of the electrode significantly increases the EDR value, i.e. an electrode with a high density presents a higher EDR value than an electrode with the same mass loading but less density.415 The EDR value also increases with increasing thickness of the electrode active materials since the pore length increases with increases in the thickness of the electrode. Another difference between ESR and EDR is that the former is independent of the current value, whereas the latter depends on testing the current value, being higher for low current densities.398
 Fig. 51  Nyquist plots of various graphite G31 electrodes with (A) the same mass loading (5.8 mg cm−2), but different densities in the range from 1.01 to 1.60 g cm−3. P, the electrode porosity, is a dimensionless parameter indicating the fraction of void volume in the electrode, (B) the same porosity (P = 0.54) but different thicknesses from 22 to 150 μm. Adapted with permission from ref. 415, copyright 2016, Elsevier. 

RC time constant
The RC time constant can be provided from: (209)In eqn (209), the RC product is the capacitive time constant and is denoted by τ. For a capacitor (RC circuit) that is initially fully charged, the voltage across the capacitor is Vmax, and q = qtotal. After every τ, the charge falls by a factor of 1/e = 0.37 relative to its value at the start of the interval. Thus, after one time constant, q = 1–0.37 = 0.63 of its initial value, indicating that at the given time interval, just 63% of the total charge can be achieved. After five time constants, the supercapacitor is said to be fully charged.390 The time constant can also be obtained from the Bode plot. The time constant can be generally expressed as:423,424 (210)At φ = 45 degrees the real and imaginary parts of the impedance are equal (i.e. the voltages across the capacitor and resistor are equal). At this point, the characteristic time constant, τ0, is calculated using eqn (211), where f0 is the frequency at an angle of −45°. (211)The time constant is a nice demonstration of the ultra-high rate charge and discharge characteristics of EDLCs (Fig. 52).
 Fig. 52  (a) Nyquist plots of two titanium nitride (TiN) devices derived from 20 and 50 nm atomic layer deposition of TiO2 named TiN-20 and TiN-50, respectively. The inset shows the zoomed-in view of the Nyquist plots in the high-frequency region. (b) The Bode phase plot versus frequency of the TiN devices in comparison with a commercial electrolytic capacitor and an activated carbon (AC) supercapacitor. Adapted with permission from ref. 424, copyright 2016, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH and Co., with slight modifications. 
In summary, CV, GCD and EIS are complementary techniques for the in-depth characterization of energy storage systems. However, due to the differences in ease with which the parameters can be obtained by these techniques, the reliability and applicability of each test method for extraction of the required information are different.
Chronoamperometry/chronocoulometry (potential step methods)
As discussed earlier, many attempts have been made toward differentiating between different charge storage mechanisms. CV can provide several useful pieces of information to differentiate between the capacitive and diffusion-limited charge storage mechanisms by altering the sweep rate. Aside from the well-established CV, GCD and EIS techniques, energy storage systems can also be studied potentiostatically. The Donne research group has successfully used the prospect of these techniques (by using step potential electrochemical spectroscopy, SPECS) to effectively differentiate between capacitive and pseudocapacitive contributions to charge storage.373,425 In these techniques, the potential of the electrode is abruptly changed from the equilibrium state (Vi) to some desired value (Vf) and the output signal is recorded as a function of time. Two types of response are normally monitored: (a) the transient current as a function of time (chronoamperometry) and (b) the charge as a function of time (chronocoulometry). The SPECS method is based on applying a series of positive or negative pulses, each of equal magnitude, over the electrochemical window of the electrode active materials. After each potential step, sufficient time is given to the electrode to establish equilibrium. This slow multi-step chronoamperometry method enables electrode active materials to approach their maximum charge storage capability.
Let us model an energy storage device as an RC circuit. When the switch is closed a voltage V is imposed on the circuit and the current across the resistor and the capacitor are monitored. At open circuit (t < 0) VR and VC are zero. At t < 0 there is no charge yet residing on the capacitor. Thus, when the switch is closed V = VR and the instantaneous current, i0, is given by: (212)As time passes the double layer capacitive current decays exponentially and is given by: (213)The voltage across the capacitor (VC) rises exponentially with time and is given by: (214)As time progresses, the voltage across the resistor approaches zero, thus at t → ∞ V = VC. The long current decay observed in energy storage systems is caused by slower, diffusion-limited pseudocapacitive reactions that contribute to the high charge storage capabilities in addition to EDL charging. For a diffusion-controlled redox reaction, the i–t response is given by: (215)which is known as the Cottrell equation. Thus, when the potential goes from its initial value, Vi, to its final value, Vf, the current rises rapidly to a maximum value and then decays as a function of t1/2. If only the two processes described above were occurring, the current decay would approach zero over time. Thus, if the measured system presents a Faradaic current due to other redox reactions at the electrode surface, the residual current when t → ∞ will not be zero, which is represented as a self-discharge current, iSelf-dis.. Thus, the total current (itotal) for each potential step can be considered to be a combination of three components:373,425 (216)The capacitance of each process, calculated by integrating the corresponding current response, can also be added together to obtain the total capacitance: Ctotal = CDL + CFaradaic + CSelf-dis.(217)Fig. 53 shows a schematic representation of the chronoamperogram in which contributions from capacitive charge storage (DL + pseudocapacitance), battery-like diffusion-controlled processes, and residual current to the total capacitance of the system can be seen.
 Fig. 53  Step potential electrochemical spectroscopy (SPECS) analysis for decoupling the contribution from different sources to the overall current. Example of the step potential electrochemical spectroscopy current response for an electrolytic manganese dioxide electrode in a 0.5 M K2SO4 electrolyte with a step potential from rest potential to 1.3 V and a rest period of 300 s for equilibration. The inset displays the de-convolution of the fitted data into current from double layer capacitance of the geometric (DL geometric) and porous surface area (DL porous), as well as the current related to the diffusion-controlled processes (diffusional). Reproduced with permission from ref. 373, copyright 2018, Electrochemical Society Inc., with modification. 
The EDL process equilibrates very fast (exponentially; eqn (214)) which results in an initial sharp decay in the current, whereas equilibration of a diffusion-controlled Faradaic process progresses slowly (eqn (215)), resulting in a slower current decay over the equilibration time. If a charged energy storage device is discharged on itself while the circuit is kept open, the device can have a leakage current. The leakage current can be easily determined by chronoamperometry, which is schematically represented in Fig. 54.
 Fig. 54  Leakage current analysis. A simple equivalent circuit for a pseudocapacitor with (a) and without (b) the leakage current, and the corresponding current transient of the system in response to a potential step from Vi to Vf. 
Chronocoulometric data actually come from the integrated chronoamperometric response, where: (218)We can integrate the Cottrell relationship to give: (219)However, the total charge, qtotal, consists of two other components: qdl due to charging the double layer, and qads = nFAΓ, due to adsorption of electroactive species on the electrode surfaces, i.e.: (220)Since the latter two terms are time independent, a plot of qtotalvs. t1/2 gives a straight line with the extrapolated intercept at t = 0 due to qdl + qads. Chronocoulometry provides practically the same information that is provided by chronoamperometry with several important experimental advantages. First, the latter part of the transient response can be detected more accurately. Second, integration of the current eliminates random noise as well as the time-independent current. Third, contributions from the double layer and any pseudocapacitance on the surface to the overall charge passed as a function of time can be discriminated from those due to the diffusing redox reactions on the electrode surfaces.
Challenges of normalization
As mentioned before, the charge storage ability of supercapacitors is normalized to different metrics, mainly mass, volume and area. The resulting capacitance, C, is named gravimetric or specific capacitance (F g−1), volumetric capacitance (F cm−3), and areal capacitance (F cm−2), respectively. However, sometimes C is normalized by the weight or volume of the active materials, but at other times by the weight or volume of the whole device. On the other hand, there are two types of area-normalized capacitance: (1) the true area normalized capacitance, and (2) the geometric area normalized capacitance. The true areal capacitance, which is the well-known EDLC, is actually the ratio of the capacitance to the specific surface area of the materials. The true area normalized capacitance is a difficult metric to obtain due to the variability in surface area measurements, especially in the case of porous active materials. Thus, inconsistencies arise when researchers prefer one type of normalization in advance, but use a different one when the time of implementation arrives. Moreover, the reported value for the capacitance strongly depends on the evaluation technique (CV or GCD), charging rate, the maximum operating potential window, as well as the mode of charge and discharge (i.e. constant current or constant voltage), in GCD. The lack of a consistent normalization parameter along with discrepancies among the results of different electrochemical testing methods makes comparisons between different energy storage devices rather challenging. Supercapacitors can deliver more energy than state-of-the-art batteries can release in short timeframes (<10 s). Thus, supercapacitors are high power energy storage devices with virtually unlimited cyclability, but generally suffer from lower energy compared to batteries. Trying to boost the energy of supercapacitors usually comes at the cost of turning high power supercapacitors into low energy batteries. In recent years, some researchers have argued that supercapacitors can approach or even outdistance batteries in energy performance. Such outstanding properties do not necessarily translate into real-world effectiveness, for which Gogotsi and Simon have provided a specific example (Fig. 55).2
 Fig. 55  Ragone plots for the same supercapacitors on a gravimetric (a) and volumetric (b) basis. The plots show that excellent properties of ultrathin films (with extremely high apparent specific power and energy values) will not translate to medium- and large-scale devices with thicker films and larger mass loadings. Adapted with permission from ref. 2, copyright 2011, American Association for the Advancement of Science. 
Thus, with a huge rise in the number of researchers who have focused on boosting the energy of supercapacitors as well as developing hybrid energy storage devices, the challenge of selecting an illustrative normalization parameter is more urgent than ever.426 The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) model is commonly used to calculate surface areas; however, this method only provides a qualitative measurement of the surface area of porous materials with small pores. Moreover, the entire surface area is not necessarily accessible to the electrolyte under all operational conditions. Gravimetric and volumetric energy/power densities are more common in the literature. However, considering a very porous carbon material, such as a graphene aerogel that has recently gained considerable attention, the gravimetric energy/power (normalized solely to the mass of the active material) would be extremely high. But the electrolyte fills in the pores of the aerogel and considerably increases the total weight of the material. Therefore, the volumetric energy/power density of such a material will be very low. Thus, particular attention should be paid to each normalization parameter. Preferentially, a combination of two or three normalization methods, based on mass/volume/area of both the active material and the whole device should be reported to paint the whole picture of the energy storage device in a more accurate way.
Performance metrics of energy storage devices are obtained from CV, GCD or EIS methods. It is generally accepted that a higher scan rate in CV or a higher current density in GCD results in a lower energy/power density. Zuliani et al. studied the electrochemical performance of two types of activated carbon; one derived from coconut shells and the other from fluid petroleum coke.426 The results show that the rate performance of the two samples differs so that a single correction factor or correlation cannot be used to explain the trend. Thus, to back up claims with facts, the materials/devices should be compared at a comparable scan rate or current density. Moreover, CV and GCD methods operate on fundamentally different mechanisms, thus the metrics derived from each of the methods are only comparable when the sweep rate of the CV curve and current density of the GCD profile are low. Generally, to compare various energy storage devices, it is strongly recommended that the criteria of assessment be limited to similar normalization factors to ensure a reasonable comparison. To provide a more realistic picture of the energy storage device, both the gravimetric and volumetric normalization factors should be presented. The gravimetric and volumetric energy/power not only should be presented based on the mass/volume of the active material(s), but also for the whole package, because all the energy storage devices need supporting components, such as current collectors, binders, separators, electrolytes, and casing.
The mass of active materials in supercapacitors is usually very low. The contribution of EDLC significantly diminishes with increasing mass of the active materials since the EDLC is limited to the accessible surface of the materials. Typically, in a supercapacitor, 100% of the active material contributes to energy storage, whereas in batteries the contribution is significantly lower. In a typical commercial supercapacitor based on activated carbon, the thickness of the electrode is about 100–200 μm or about 10 mg cm−2 of a carbon film. In such a case, the weight of carbon accounts for only 30% of the whole package, thus dividing by a factor of 3 to 4 is needed to extrapolate energy and power of the active material to those of the device. Such an extrapolation is only valid for electrodes with a comparable thickness or loading. Thus, mass normalizing the energy and power in a micro-supercapacitor, a thin film supercapacitor or the one with a negligible mass loading is erroneous compared to the energy normalized by the volume and area of the electrode. Since limited space is a key constraint, volumetric/areal capacitance, energy and power are more meaningful evaluation criteria.
Mechanical flexibility of electrodes and devices
The ever-growing demands for flexible electronics in applications of consumer electronics, activity trackers, implantable/wearable healthcare devices, environmental monitoring and many more require concerted mechanical flexibility in energy storage devices.77,427–430 Significant progress has therefore been made in the fabrication of bendable, stretchable, shape-memory, transparent and self-healing energy storage devices over recent years (Fig. 56).71,430–437 Flexible energy storage devices have been made as 1D fibers,433,438–440 2D films,70,300,428 and 3D foams173,441–443 with ordered porous structures. For applications in portable and wearable electronics, performance stability of the energy storage devices under different mechanical bending, knotting, twisting, compressing, and stretching conditions, as well as harsh environmental situations (including extremely high or low temperature, high humidity levels, exposure to radiation and vibration), is an important performance metric.431 The main purpose of mechanical testing in the energy storage realm is to get a qualitative sense of flexibility and to ensure that products meet or exceed the minimum requirements for modern electrical equipment. For example, a common test of a material's mechanical properties involves fracturing a cylinder of the material. The load on the cylinder is increased gradually until the test specimen breaks or fractures suddenly into two or more pieces. For example, besides a quantitative analysis, Ling et al. reported hollow MXene/PVA cylinders (6 mm in diameter and 10 mm high) made by rolling Ti3C2Tx-based films with a thickness of 4–5 μm that were capable of supporting ∼15000 times their own weight.444 However, a detailed and quantitative description of a specimen's flexibility cannot be obtained by just one such routine test. For a quantitative assessment, the mechanical properties of electrodes/devices must be evaluated using (1) stress–strain curves, (2) monitoring the in situ change in resistance of the system, (3) studying the in situ electrochemical performance of the system under different mechanical deformations, and (4) recording spectroscopic information or microscopic images of the materials at different levels of applied strains.431
 Fig. 56  Mechanical tests of the devices. (a) Schematic representation of an all-solid-state integrated supercapacitor based on a polyaniline–SWCNT–sponge. (b) The patterns for assembling four supercapacitors into one unit in series. Real-time photographs of the resultant four supercapacitors in series showing the performance of the device assembly in (c) free, (d) compressed, and (e) uncompressed states. (f) The shape memory process of a solid-state shape memory asymmetric supercapacitor induced by touching with a human wrist. PTT: phase transition temperature. (g) Schematic illustration of the preparation of an aligned CNT/Si composite fiber electrode used for a fiber-shaped Li-ion battery. (h) Schematic illustration of the fabrication process for a super-stretchy fiber-shaped Li-ion battery. Reprinted with permission from (a–e) ref. 445, copyright 2015, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH and Co., (f) ref. 446, copyright 2016, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH and Co., (g) ref. 447, copyright 2014, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH and Co., and (h) ref. 448, copyright 2014, Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Stress–strain curve
The stress–strain test is used to determine mechanical properties of a material, including the tensile, compressive, shear, or torsional strength. An object under tensile stress experiences an increase in length, whereas an object under compressive stress undergoes a decrease in length. If instead of applying a force perpendicular to the surface, we apply pairs of equal, but opposing forces on the two opposite sides of an object, we are applying a shear stress. Torsional stress is the twisting of a straight object that tends to produce rotation about the longitudinal axes of the object. While stress measures the applied force per unit area of the specimen, strain measures the relative change in length caused by the applied force. The stress, σ (N m−2 or Pa), is defined as: (221)where F is the tensile/compressive/shear force (N), and A0 is the initial cross-sectional area of the specimen (m2). In the case of tensile and compressive strains, which are normal strains in response to normal stresses, the mathematical expression is the change in length divided by the original length of the specimen and is typically expressed as a percentage. (222)where ε is the normal strain, ΔL is the change in specimen's length (L − L0), and L0 is the initial length of the specimen (since both quantities have the dimension length, ε as their ratio is dimensionless). Note that under biaxial tensile/compressive strain elongation/compression occurs along two dimensions of the material. Therefore, the percentage of strain should be reported for both the x and y dimensions, or simply the areal strain of the sample can be reported as: (223)where Δa is the change in the area, and a0 is the area in an unstrained state.449 An important aspect of mechanical testing is the preparation of the proper specimen. A typical tensile specimen has enlarged shoulders for gripping and a gage section in between (Fig. 57a). The reduced cross-sectional area of the gage section localizes stress and ensures that deformation and failure consistently occur in this region. The distances between the ends of the gage section and the shoulders should be long enough (at least as great as the diameter) so that the shoulder sections do not constrain deformation within the gage section, and the length of the gage region should be at least four times the diameter.450 In compressive tests, specimens are typically cylinders. For analytical purposes, σ is plotted against ε during a tensile test experiment. Fig. 57b displays a typical stress–strain curve. For most materials, the initial part of the stress–strain curve is linear. The slope of this linear region is called the elastic modulus or Young's modulus (N m−2 or Pa), and the area under the elastic portion of the curve represents the elastic strain energy in units of J m−3. (224)Measurement of the stress at which plastic deformation first occurs is sometimes difficult. The onset of plasticity is therefore usually described by an offset yield strength, which can be obtained by constructing a straight line parallel to the Young's modulus region but offset by ε = 0.002 or 0.2%. The intersection of this line and the stress–strain profile defines the 0.2% yield point. Sometimes the offset values are 0.1 or 0.5%. In any case, the criterion should be clearly stated so that the user can interpret the data accurately.
 Fig. 57  Stress–Strain plot. (a) A typical tensile specimen, showing a reduced gage section and enlarged shoulders. For a proper test, the length of the transition region, x, should be equal to or greater than the diameter, and the total length of the reduced section should be at least four times the diameter. (b) A typical stress–strain curve for a material undergoing tensile strain testing. 
Stress–strain profiles have recently been utilized by many scientists as a method for quantitative assessment of the energy storage materials/devices.443,444,451–456 For example, Di et al. reported a flexible CNT film using aligned few-walled nanotubes that exhibited tensile strengths of up to ∼2 GPa and Young's modulus of up to ∼90 GPa, which is remarkably superior to other types of carbon-based films, including commercial graphite foils (tensile strengths of up to ∼15 MPa, Young's modulus of up to ∼3 GPa), buckypapers (tensile strengths of up to 80 MPa, Young's modulus of up to 14 GPa), and graphene-related papers (tensile strengths of up to ∼300 MPa, Young's modulus of up to 42 GPa).457 Zhang's research group reported size-controlled MoS2/graphene hybrid aerogels with a Young's modulus of up to 59.2 kPa and recoverable deformability of 90% elastic strain, indicating their applicability in flexible Li-ion batteries.458 Seo et al. reported mechanically robust and highly ionic conductive gels based on random copolymers that exhibited both a large Young's modulus of ∼0.1 MPa and an ionic conductivity of ∼1.2 mS cm−1 at room temperature.459 Wang et al. also reported a SWCNT-PANI film electrode with tensile strength and Young's modulus of 10.9 and 655 MPa, respectively.460

In situ resistance measurements

In situ changes in resistance have also been employed to test the flexibility of electrodes.443,461,462 For example, we reported 3D macroporous laser-scribed graphene (LSG)–MnO2 electrodes and evaluated the foldability of the LSG–MnO2 electrodes by in situ measurements of the electrode resistance under successive bending cycles (Fig. 58).173 The change in resistance of the electrode was negligible up to a bending radius of 5.0 mm and was completely recovered after straightening no matter whether the bending was positive (convex) or negative (concave). Notably, after 1000 cycles of bending and straightening in a concave configuration, with a radius of curvature of 5.0 mm, the resistance was increased by only ∼2.8%, demonstrating excellent mechanical integrity under large mechanical deformation. This is in contrast to MnO2 by itself, which undergoes serious strain-induced performance degradation because of its rigid structure.
 Fig. 58  Fabrication and characterization of flexible LSG/MnO2 electrodes. (a) Schematic representation of the fabrication process of the LSG–MnO2 electrodes. (b) Variation of the resistance of an LSG–MnO2 electrode as a function of bending radius both in concave and convex configuration. (c) The change in resistance of an LSG–MnO2 electrode under repeated bending cycles for a concave bending radius of 5 mm. The inset photograph shows the flexibility of an LSG–MnO2 electrode. Adapted with permission from ref. 173, copyright 2015, National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America. 
In another study, we introduced a supercapacitor based on polyaniline-functionalized carbon cloth.27 To test the flexibility of the device, we monitored the change in electrical resistance of the device under different bending angles from flat to bent at 180°. We also investigated the performance of the device under repeated bending cycles to a bending angle of 180°. Notably, we observed 96% capacity retention with only a slight decrease in resistance (<4%) after bending at an angle of 180° for 1000 cycles. It should be noted that when the bending radius is large relative to the device/electrode thickness, besides bending angle, the radius of curvature should also be reported to give a real picture of the device/electrode bendability. If a device has a radius of curvature between zero to twice the thickness of the device, it can be regarded as a foldable device.

In situ electrochemical study
Mechanical tests of the energy storage materials/devices are generally performed by recording electrochemical performances under different mechanical deformations. For example, Zang et al. reported supercapacitor electrodes based on crumpled graphene paper that exhibited extremely high stretchability with a uniaxial strain of ∼300% and a biaxial strain of 200% × 200%, which is equivalent to 800% areal strain (Fig. 59).453 They have demonstrated that the capacitances of the electrodes are insensitive to large deformations of the electrodes. The Hamedi research group introduced a flexible and compressible three-dimensional supercapacitor based on layer-by-layer self-assembly of interdigitated thin films that is fully functional at a bending angle of 90° and compressions up to 75%.442 To demonstrate this flexibility, in situ CV experiments were performed while the device was under operation.
 Fig. 59  Stretchability test of the graphene paper. Photographs of the procedure for crumpling graphene paper (a–c) and the corresponding SEM images (d–f): photograph (a) and SEM image (d) of a flat graphene paper bonded on a biaxially pre-stretched (εpre1 = εpre2 = 400%) elastomer film. Photographs (b and c) and SEM images (e and f) of the microscopic patterns formed in the graphene paper after the biaxially pre-stretched elastomeric substrate is uniaxially (b and e) and biaxially (c and f) relaxed to parallel ridges (e) and crumpled patterns (f), respectively. CV curves at a scan rate of 50 mV s−1 (g) and gravimetric capacitance at different charge/discharge current densities from 0.5 to 80 A g−1 (h) of the crumpled-graphene-paper electrodes subjected to uniaxial strains of 0%, 100%, 200%, and 300%. (i) CV curves of the crumpled-graphene-paper electrodes subjected to biaxial strains of 0% × 0% and 200% × 200%. Adapted with permission from ref. 453, copyright 2014, Springer Nature. 
Spectroscopic and microscopic characterization
A few other methods have also been used for studying the mechanical flexibility of materials. For example, Lee et al. estimated the Young's modulus of single- and bilayer graphene by Raman spectroscopy as 2.4 ± 0.4 and 2.0 ± 0.5 TPa, respectively,463 while the Hone research group used atomic force microscopy (AFM) nanoindentation and measured the Young's modulus of a freestanding monolayer of graphene as 1 TPa.464
Conclusions and perspective
Personal electronic devices and transportation technologies are now more dependent on energy storage systems than at any previous time in history, with a strong demand for enhanced energy and power densities. Therefore, a vast variety of advanced electrode active materials for batteries, supercapacitors, and hybrid energy storage systems have been reported. However, when critically evaluating energy storage systems under realistic conditions, it appears that in some cases, the characteristics assigned to an energy storage system (especially those based on the gravimetric performance of the active materials) have been overestimated. This is because of the low mass loadings of the electrode active materials and the dead volume in cells that prevent material-based gravimetric characteristics to be translated into realistic device-level volumetric performances. These common challenges of energy storage systems call for an attempt towards clarifying the importance of a balanced view of the active materials and devices.
In this Review, a comprehensive survey of the basic principles of energy storage systems and the protocols for their evaluation are presented. We have briefly described the physico-chemical principles of conventional capacitors, electrical double-layer capacitors, pseudocapacitors, hybrid energy storage devices, and batteries. In parallel with improvements in advanced engineering and the development of high-performance electrode active materials, a fundamental understanding and further development of the criteria for their proper evaluation are also needed to meet the challenges of new energy storage technologies. In this continuum, from conventional capacitors to supercapacitors to batteries, (super)capacitors are generally exploited to meet high-power demands, while batteries are generally employed to meet high-energy needs. Unlike conventional capacitors, the concomitant Faradaic reactions in pseudocapacitors and hybrid energy storage devices result in nonlinear galvanostatic charge–discharge behavior, and cyclic voltammograms that significantly deviate from the rectangular-shaped voltammograms expected for capacitors. Characterizations of pseudocapacitors and hybrid energy storage devices with metrics of capacitors or electric-double layer supercapacitors sometimes lead to false negative or positive results for the energy content and power performance of the device. We have emphasized here that the metrics for evaluation of energy storage devices vary depending on the type, since each energy storage device sets out specific characteristics with special use cases.
While desirable advancements have been achieved for the evaluation of energy storage devices, drawbacks still exist. For example, when pseudocapacitive materials are used in an energy storage device, the mass loading and thickness of the electrode active materials must be indicated. When the mass loading or thickness of the electrode active material is very low, or the charge–discharge processes are conducted using a very low current density, the energy performance of the material may be erroneously regarded as high, while finding a correction factor for this overestimation may not be easy. In this regard, the combination of computational modeling and experimental investigations should become an effective method for extrapolating and predicting the properties of both materials and devices. We hope that this Review will provide a useful contribution to the energy-storage community as a roadmap for ameliorating the hurdles for evaluating electrochemical energy storage systems.

Table Content:

Abolhassan Noori
	Abolhassan Noori received his PhD under the supervision of Professor A. Abbaspour from Shiraz University in 2011. During his PhD he spent 6 months in the group of (late) Prof. Marco Mascini at the University of Florence, Italy, as a Visiting Student Researcher, working on the implementation of nanostructured materials in biosensors. After that, he was awarded five one-year fellowships sponsored by the Tarbiat Modares University (TMU) Research Council and another one from the Iran National Science Foundation (INSF) to pursue his postdoctoral research in the laboratory of Professor Mir F. Mousavi. In 2015, he began pursuing Electrochemical Energy Storage (EES) systems. Recently, Dr Noori joined the Department of Chemistry at TMU as a Research Associate Professor, working with Prof. Mousavi to continue expanding his expertise and knowledge in energy storage/conversion systems. He is a fellow of the Iranian Chemical Society (ICS) and Iranian Nanotechnology Society (INS). His current research interests focus on the rational design of nanostructured materials, bioinspired from nature, to solve problems in bioanalytical chemistry and materials science, ranging from challenges in targeted drug delivery to energy-related applications.

Maher F. El-Kady
	Maher F. El-Kady is a postdoctoral research fellow at UCLA and chief technology officer at Nanotech Energy, Inc. For more than 12 years, Dr El-Kady has been studying the chemistry and physics of materials with applications in energy storage, electronic devices, medical and healthcare devices, chemical sensors and biosensors, nanogenerators, solar cells, water splitting, hydrogen energy, water remediation and environmental monitoring. This work resulted in 16 US and international patents issued and pending and 40+ papers in prestigious journals. His groundbreaking work on graphene supercapacitors led to the development of Nanotech Energy, Inc. where he is leading a team of scientists and engineers for developing more powerful batteries, supercapacitors, printed electronics, nanomaterials, conductive inks and adhesives. He has received numerous awards for his research including the UCLA Herbert Newby McCoy Award, the Lindau Graduate Student Award, the Amgen Postdoctoral Award, the Cairo University Presidential Award for Excellence in Research and the 2012 Excellence in Research Award from the Government of Egypt.

Mohammad S. Rahmanifar
	M. S. Rahmanifar received his BSc (1995) from Kharazmi University, and MSc (1998) and PhD (2003) from Tarbiat Modarres University under the supervision of Prof. M. F. Mousavi. He then began his academic career at Shahed University in 2005 and was promoted to Associate Professor with tenure starting in 2017. His research interests focus on the design and fabrication of electrochemical power sources (EPSs) and the electrochemistry of materials and their application in EPSs.

Richard B. Kaner
	Richard Kaner received his PhD from the University of Pennsylvania in 1984 working with Prof. Alan MacDiarmid (Nobel Laureate 2000, deceased). After postdoctoral research at Berkeley, he joined the University of California, Los Angeles (UCLA), in 1987, earned tenure in 1991, became a Distinguished Professor in 2012 and was appointed to the Dr Myung Ki Hong Endowed Chair in Materials Innovation in 2017. He has published over 400 papers in top peer reviewed journals and holds more than 40 U.S. patents. According to the latest Thomson-Reuters rankings, he is among the world's most highly cited authors. Professor Kaner has received awards from the Dreyfus, Fulbright, Guggenheim, Packard and Sloan Foundations along with the Materials Research Society Medal, the Royal Society of Chemistry Centenary Prize, and the American Chemical Society's Buck-Whitney Research Award, Tolman Medal and Award in the Chemistry of Materials for his work on refractory materials including new synthetic routes to ceramics, intercalation compounds, superhard metals, graphene and conducting polymers. He has been elected a Fellow of the American Association for the Advancement of Science (AAAS), the American Chemical Society (ACS), the European Academy of Sciences (EurASc), the Materials Research Society (MRS) and the Royal Society of Chemistry (FRSC).

Mir F. Mousavi
	Mir F. Mousavi received a PhD from Shiraz University in 1992. He then joined the Chemistry Department of Tarbiat Modares University (TMU) in 1992 as an Assistant Professor, became an Associate Professor in 1998, and was promoted to Full Professor in 2002. He has been listed as one of the Leading Scientists and Engineers in the OIC Member States in 2006, and an ISI (ESI) scientist (Top 1% Most Cited Scientist) in 2007. Prof. Mousavi has been recognized as the First Laureate in Modern Technology at the 14th Annual Razi International Research Festival in 2008, and has received many awards including the ISESCO Award in 2008, the Iranian Nanotechnology Society (INS) Award in 2009, the Iranian Chemical Society (ICS) Award in 2009, and the ICS Award for Distinguished Professor of Analytical Chemistry in 2011. He is a member of many academies, including the American Chemical Society (ACS), the International Society of Electrochemistry (ISE), the Iranian Chemical Society (ICS), the Iranian Nanotechnology Society (INS), the Iran Science Elites Federation (ISEF), and was elected as a Regional Representative for ISE from 2007 to 2012. Prof. Mousavi has been a Visiting Professor in Prof. Richard Kaner's laboratory at UCLA from 2013–2014. His current research interests involve advanced nanostructured materials and technologies for energy storage and conversion devices, including rechargeable batteries, supercapacitors, as well as hybrid systems, along with the applications of nanomaterials in nanobiotechnology.

 	Fig. 3  A hydroelectric power plant is analogous to an electrical energy storage system. Water from the upper reservoir flows through the pipes to turn a turbine generator, the same way that electrons flow through the wires of a battery from the negative pole to the positive one to produce light. The inset shows the energy diagram of the devices. The symbols c and a are the electrochemical potentials of the electrons (i.e. the Fermi energy of the electrons) of the negative pole (the anode during the discharge step) and the positive pole (the cathode during the discharge step), respectively. Vg is the voltage window of the electrolyte. VOC is the open-circuit voltage of the device.

 	Fig. 5  System theory as a way of studying the energy storage process in its entirety. In such a system, the input could be the voltage or current that is applied to the energy storage device and the output could be the current or voltage that can be readily monitored. The feedback of the output to the input could provide information about the system under study, e.g. the minimum and maximum cut-off voltages.

 	Fig. 10  Charging an R–C circuit. R–C circuit for t < 0 (a) and t ≥ 0 (b). At t < 0 the switch is open and no current flows through the circuit and the charge on the plates is zero.  	Fig. 17  (A) A plot of areal capacitance normalized by BET specific surface area vs. average pore size for different carbon samples. All the samples were tested in the same electrolyte (NEt4BF4 in acetonitrile). The traditional view is based on an assumption that the normalized capacitance continually decreases with decreasing pore size. The results presented in this figure show that the normalized capacitance decreases with decreasing pore size until a critical value is reached after which a huge increase in the capacitance is observed.  	Fig. 34  Schematic illustration of self-discharge mechanisms: (a) charge redistribution is the main mechanism of self-discharge in EDLCs. Movement of ions between the Stern layer and the electrolyte and subsequently across the electrolyte due to movement of charge through the electrode active materials causes self-discharge in supercapacitors. (b) Self-discharge in redox pseudocapacitors, as well as in hybrid systems, is mainly caused by redox shuttling between charged redox species in solution (b, top magnified panel) or via cross-diffusion of charged redox couples between the oppositely charged electrodes (b, bottom magnified panel). Panel b is adapted with permission from ref. 317, copyright 2017, American Chemical Society.

(c) The change in resistance of an LSG–MnO2 electrode under repeated bending cycles for a concave bending radius of 5 mm. The inset photograph shows the flexibility of an LSG–MnO2 electrode.  	Fig. 59  Stretchability test of the graphene paper. Photographs of the procedure for crumpling graphene paper (a–c) and the corresponding SEM images (d–f): photograph (a) and SEM image (d) of a flat graphene paper bonded on a biaxially pre-stretched (εpre1 = εpre2 = 400%) elastomer film. Photographs (b and c) and SEM images (e and f) of the microscopic patterns formed in the graphene paper after the biaxially pre-stretched elastomeric substrate is uniaxially (b and e) and biaxially (c and f) relaxed to parallel ridges (e) and crumpled patterns (f), respectively. CV curves at a scan rate of 50 mV s−1 (g) and gravimetric capacitance at different charge/discharge current densities from 0.5 to 80 A g−1 (h) of the crumpled-graphene-paper electrodes subjected to uniaxial strains of 0%, 100%, 200%, and 300%. (i) CV curves of the crumpled-graphene-paper electrodes subjected to biaxial strains of 0% × 0% and 200% × 200%. Adapted with permission from ref. 453, copyright 2014, Springer Nature.
